Middle Term Must Be the Cause for Certainty
Author critiques use of non-necessary premises and explains that the middle term must be the cause for true certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism's major premise lacks causal necessity and explain why it fails to produce genuine certainty.

Core Idea
Certainty requires that the middle term be the cause, not just a true premise.

This passage from al-Mantiq al-Kabir examines a common error: assuming that any true and necessary premise yields certainty. The author shows that the major premise's necessity must be causal—known by the cause—or the conclusion lacks real certainty. He illustrates this with the example of proving humanity from movement. We will dissect the logical structure and the condition for demonstrative certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fallacy claimed
The author states that using accepted (maqbulat) or prevalent (mashhurat) premises in seeking certainty is a fallacy. These premises are taken on authority or common belief, not from necessity.
2
Extract the example syllogism
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism: 'This human is an animal because he moves' (minor: this human moves; major: everything that moves is an animal; conclusion: this human is an animal).
The middle term is 'movement'.
3
Analyze the necessity of the major premise
Is the major premise 'everything moving is an animal' necessary? The author distinguishes between necessity in Qiyas (formal) and necessity in Burhan (causal). Without causal knowledge, the certainty is not real.
4
Distinguish between Qiyas and Burhan
A syllogism only yields certainty if it reduces to a demonstrative form (qiyas burhani) where the middle term is the cause ('illa). Otherwise, it remains merely dialectical or formal.
5
Apply the causal condition
Determine what makes the middle term the cause: in the example, 'movement' can be an essential accident of both man and animal, serving as a causal link. The syllogism becomes demonstrative due to this causal connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يستراب في أن استعمال المقبولات والمشهورات وأمثالها في طلب اليقين مغالطة، والمقدمات الصادقة التي تلزم منها النتائج الصادقة لا يلزم أن تكون تلك النتائج يقينية بل إنما تكون يقينية إذا كانت تلك المقدمات ضرورية مناسبة للمطلوب. فإذا قلتَ مثلا : إن هذا الإنسان حيوان لأنه متحرك، وكل متحرك حيوان؛ فإذا لم يتحرك بطل العلم الحاصل بتوسط الحركة فلا يدرى أنه حيوان أو غير حيوان فإن قيل بل العلم الحاصل باق كما كان؛ وإن لم يبق الحد الأوسط فإن قولنا وكل متحرك حيوان معناه كل شيء هو متحرك في الجملة فهو حيوان دائما ما دامت الذوات الموضوعة الحركة موجودة فتكون الصغرى وجودية والكبرى ضرورية، والنتيجة منهما ضرورية كما مر . فنقول هذا إنما يفيد اليقين لرجوعه بالقوة إلى قياس برهاني وإلا لم يفد اليقين. فإن الكبرى الضرورية ضرورتها على نحو ضرورة كتاب القياس لا على نحو ضرورة كتاب البرهان؛ فإن قولنا كل متحرّك بالضرورة حيوان فلا يخلو من أن يكون عرفانه بالعلة أو لا بالعلة فإن لم يكن بالعلة واللمية لم يكن اليقين حقيقيًا كليا، وإن كان بالعلة فإنه إنما اكتسب اليقين بقياس العلة؛ وهذا الشيء يمكن أن يكون من الأعراض الذاتية بالإنسان من وجه، وبالحيوان من وجه على ما عرف من قبل فيكون إنما صار هذا القياس برهانيًا لأن الأوسط
There is no doubt that using accepted premises and prevalent ones and their like in seeking certainty is a fallacy. True premises from which true conclusions follow do not necessitate that those conclusions be certain; rather they are certain only if those premises are necessary and appropriate to the demand. For example, if you say: This human is an animal because he moves, and everything that moves is an animal. If he does not move, the knowledge obtained through movement is invalidated, so it is not known whether he is an animal or not. If it is said: Rather the knowledge obtained remains as it was, even if the middle term does not remain, for our statement 'everything that moves is an animal' means that everything that moves in general is always an animal as long as the subjects possessing movement exist, so the minor premise is existential and the major is necessary, and the conclusion from them is necessary as has passed. We say: This only yields certainty because it potentially reduces to a demonstrative syllogism; otherwise it would not yield certainty. For the necessary major premise, its necessity is on the pattern of necessity in the book of Qiyas, not on the pattern of necessity in the book of Burhan. For our statement 'everything moving is necessarily an animal' – it is not free from being known by the cause or not by the cause. If it is not by the cause and the reason (limiyya), the certainty is not real and universal. If it is by the cause, then certainty is acquired by the syllogism of the cause. And this thing can be among the essential accidents of man from one aspect, and of animal from another, as known before, so this syllogism becomes demonstrative because the middle term [is the cause].
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage critically examines the conditions for certainty in demonstration. The author first states that using accepted or prevalent premises in seeking certainty is a fallacy (mughalata). Even true premises do not guarantee certain conclusions unless they are necessary and appropriate to the demand. An example is given: 'This human is an animal because he moves' and 'Everything that moves is an animal.' If the minor premise (this human is moving) is existential and the major is necessary, the conclusion might seem necessary. However, the author argues that such reasoning only yields certainty if it can be reduced to a demonstrative syllogism. The key condition is that the major premise's necessity must be known by way of the cause ('illa). If the link between movement and being animal is not causal, the certainty is not real and universal. Only a syllogism where the middle term is the cause provides genuine demonstrative certainty (qiyas burhani). The passage concludes that the middle term can be an essential accident of both subject and predicate, making the syllogism demonstrative.

Key Terms
مقبولات
مشهورات
حد أوسط
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The major premise must be necessary.
B) The middle term must be the cause ('illa).
C) The premises must be true.
D) The conclusion must be necessary.

Key Takeaway
Real certainty in demonstration requires that the middle term be the cause ('illa), not just a true premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Necessary Premises and Genuine Certainty
Author explains the condition for certainty: premises must be necessary and known through their cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given syllogism's major premise meets the conditions for producing genuine certainty (yaqin haqiqi) by checking if it is necessary and known through its cause.

Core Idea
Certainty requires premises that are not just true, but necessary and understood through their cause.

In this passage, the author warns that true premises do not automatically yield certain conclusions. For genuine certainty, the premises must be necessary (daruriyya) and their necessity must be known through the cause ('illa). This lesson unpacks that condition using the example from the text.

Guided Path
1
Find the condition for certainty
Read the passage and locate where the author states that true premises can yield true conclusions but not necessarily certain ones. Look for the line 'المقدمات الصادقة التي تلزم منها النتائج الصادقة لا يلزم أن تكون تلك النتائج يقينية'.
This sets up the problem: truth is not enough.
2
Identify the required property of premises
The author then specifies what makes premises yield certainty: 'بل إنما تكون يقينية إذا كانت تلك المقدمات ضرورية مناسبة للمطلوب'. So premises must be necessary (ضرورية) and appropriate (مناسبة) for the sought conclusion.
Necessity here means the premise is always true, not contingent.
3
Understand the cause condition
Later, the author refines this: the necessity of the major premise must be known by cause (بالعلة). He says: 'فإن لم يكن بالعلة واللمية لم يكن اليقين حقيقيًا كليا' – if not known by cause, the certainty is not real and universal.
This means we must understand why the premise is necessary, not just assert it.
4
Test the example
Apply the condition to the example: 'هذا الإنسان حيوان لأنه متحرك، وكل متحرك حيوان'. Does this syllogism yield certainty? Only if the major premise 'كل متحرك حيوان' is necessary and its necessity is known by cause (e.g., movement is an essential accident of animal). Otherwise, the certainty is imperfect.
The author says it reverts potentially to a demonstrative syllogism.

upon the necessary thing insofar as it is necessary; and it is not necessary. As for how it may be reflected so that certainty is obtained from them, that will be known later. They said: Every statement that produces a necessary thing and is not necessary, then the opponent may say that the antecedent which you posited is not always existent, so what follows from it is not always existent; since it is not necessary that it be always existent. This is among the things that have a point if the claim is to prove a thing necessarily, but if not, then it has no point. There is no doubt that using accepted opinions, well-known opinions, and the like in seeking certainty is a fallacy. True premises from which true conclusions follow do not necessarily make those conclusions certain; rather, they are certain only if those premises are necessary and appropriate for the sought conclusion. For example, if you say: This human is an animal because it moves, and everything that moves is an animal; then if it does not move, the knowledge obtained through movement becomes invalid, so it is not known whether it is an animal or not. And if it is said: Rather, the acquired knowledge remains as it was, even if the middle term does not remain, because our statement 'everything that moves is an animal' means that everything that is moving in general is an animal always as long as the substances subject to movement exist; so the minor premise is existential and the major is necessary, and the conclusion from them is necessary as previously. We say: This only yields certainty because it reverts potentially to a demonstrative syllogism; otherwise it would not yield certainty. For the necessary major's necessity is in the manner of the necessity of the book of syllogism, not in the manner of the necessity of the book of demonstration. For our statement 'everything that moves is necessarily an animal' cannot escape either being known by cause or not by cause. If it is not by cause and the reason why, then the certainty is not real and universal. If it is by cause, then certainty is acquired only by a syllogism of the cause. And this thing can be among the essential accidents of human in one respect, and of animal in another, as was known before. So this syllogism becomes demonstrative only because the middle term in it is an essential accident, namely movement; so it reverts with the two premises potentially to two premises whose major is necessary. And it does no harm if this syllogism is not actually a demonstrative syllogism, for certainty does not come from its being actual, but rather certainty occurs because of its being potential. Just as it is possible to produce true conclusions from false premises, so too it is possible to produce necessary conclusions from non-necessary premises. And just as the true conclusion there is not true by virtue of the very syllogism but by virtue of its being true in itself, so too the necessary conclusion there is not necessary by virtue of consequence but by virtue of its being necessary in itself. And just as there, after sensing the premises, one may doubt and not know whether the conclusion is true or false, even if it is true in itself, so too there one may doubt and not know whether the conclusion is necessary or non-necessary unless its necessity is known from another aspect that appears with those premises (namely their force) or does not appear from them but from other premises. And just as there, a false conclusion cannot be produced from true premises, so too here, a non-necessary conclusion cannot be produced from necessary premises. Then accidental premises, even if they do not produce something necessary, may still produce necessarily. And there is a difference between what produces a necessary thing and what produces necessarily. For every syllogism produces necessarily, but not every syllogism produces a necessary thing. So if it produces necessarily but does not produce a necessary thing, it is not devoid of benefit; rather it has two benefits: [1] One of them is the knowledge of the existence of something, even if not certain; and there is a difference between absolute knowledge and certain knowledge. [2] The second is refuting the opponent when he permits the admission of the premise; and this is far from the method of demonstration, for demonstration does not depend on the opponent's admission of the premise but on the Truth's admission of it and that it be necessary; and they are not necessary in the manner taken in demonstration unless their predicates, along with their necessity, are essential in one of the two ways of essential as previously. Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the conditions under which premises produce certainty (yaqin) in demonstrative reasoning. The author begins by distinguishing between statements that are necessary in themselves and those that are not. He warns against relying on accepted opinions (maqbulat) and well-known opinions (mashhurat) when seeking certainty, labeling such usage as fallacious. He then addresses a key point: true premises may yield true conclusions, but those conclusions are not automatically certain. They are certain only if the premises are necessary (daruriyya) and appropriate for the desired conclusion.

The author provides an example: the argument 'This human is an animal because it moves, and everything that moves is an animal.' The issue is that if the human stops moving, the knowledge based on movement disappears, so one cannot be certain the human remains an animal. A possible objection is that the major premise 'everything that moves is an animal' is understood as a universal necessary statement: any moving thing is an animal as long as its substance exists. However, the author responds that such a syllogism only yields certainty if it is potentially a demonstrative syllogism (qiyas burhani). The major premise's necessity must be of the kind studied in the book of demonstration (Kitab al-Burhan), not merely in the book of syllogism (Kitab al-Qiyas). In other words, the necessity must be known through its cause ('illa), otherwise the certainty is not real and universal.

The author notes that movement can be an essential accident ('arad dhati) of both human (as an animal) and animal (as a species of body). So if the middle term (movement) is an essential accident, the syllogism is demonstrative in potentiality, even if not in actuality. Certainty arises from this potentiality.

A parallel is then drawn between producing true conclusions from false premises and producing necessary conclusions from non-necessary premises. In both cases, the value of the conclusion does not come from the syllogism's form but from the conclusion's own nature. The author makes four numbered comparisons: (1) Just as false premises can yield true conclusions, non-necessary premises can yield necessary conclusions. (2) The truth/necessity of the conclusion in such cases is due to itself, not the inference. (3) One may doubt the conclusion's truth/necessity even if it is true/necessary in itself, unless its necessity is known from another source. (4) True premises cannot yield a false conclusion, and necessary premises cannot yield a non-necessary conclusion.

The author then clarifies that accidental premises (muqaddimat 'aradiyya) cannot produce a necessary thing (i.e., a necessary conclusion), but they do produce necessarily (i.e., the inference is still valid). He distinguishes between 'producing a necessary thing' (intaj daruri) and 'producing necessarily' (intaj bi-al-darura). Every syllogism produces necessarily, but not every syllogism produces a necessary conclusion. The benefit of a syllogism that produces necessarily without producing a necessary thing is twofold: (1) it gives absolute knowledge (not certain) of the existence of something, and (2) it can be used to refute an opponent who admits the premises. However, the latter is far from demonstration, which depends on the truth itself, not on the opponent's concession. For demonstration, the predicates must be essential (dhati) as previously defined.

Finally, the author states that if the middle term is essential to the minor term, then the syllogism can be demonstrative. This passage is a careful analysis of the epistemic conditions for certainty, emphasizing the need for necessity and essential predication.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition must the major premise fulfill for a syllogism to produce genuine certainty (yaqin haqiqi)?
A) It must be true and universally quantified.
B) It must be necessary and known through its cause (bi al-illa).
C) It must be admitted by the opponent.
D) It must be derived from sense perception.

Key Takeaway
Certainty in demonstration requires premises that are not only necessary but known through their cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a demonstrative syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., 'Prayer is obligatory because it is commanded, and everything commanded is obligatory') and explain whether the major premise 'everything commanded is obligatory' is necessary and known through its cause. If not, what would be required to make it certain?

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced
10 min
Essential Accident and Potential Demonstration
Author explains how an essential accident as middle term makes a syllogism potentially demonstrative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a middle term is an essential accident ('arad dhati) and explain how it makes a syllogism potentially demonstrative, yielding certainty even if not actually demonstrative.

Core Idea
An essential accident as middle term gives a syllogism potential demonstrative power.

The passage explains that movement can be an essential accident for both human and animal. When the middle term is an essential accident, the syllogism becomes demonstrative in potentiality (bi al-quwwa), even if not in actuality. This is why the example 'this human is an animal because it moves' can yield certainty. Let's explore this concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in the example
In the syllogism 'This human is an animal because it moves, and everything that moves is an animal', the middle term is 'movement' (الحركة). It is the term that connects the minor term (human) and the major term (animal).
The middle term is the cause of the conclusion.
2
Determine if it is an essential accident
The author says movement can be an essential accident (عرضي ذاتي) for human in one respect and for animal in another. An essential accident is a property that necessarily belongs to the subject but is not part of its definition. Movement necessarily belongs to animal (as a living body) and to human (as a type of animal).
Essential accidents are predicated of their subjects necessarily.
3
Understand potential vs actual demonstration
The author states that this syllogism yields certainty because it reverts potentially to a demonstrative syllogism. Even if the syllogism is not actually (بالفعل) demonstrative, the certainty comes from its potential (بالقوة). This means the middle term is such that it could be used in a fully demonstrative syllogism if we explicate the causal connection.
Potential demonstration relies on the middle term being an essential accident.
4
Apply the concept to another example
In usul al-fiqh, the 'illa (effective cause) for a ruling is an essential accident. For example, the prohibition of wine is due to intoxication (الإسكار). Intoxication is an essential accident of wine (it necessarily causes intoxication). A syllogism: 'Wine is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden, therefore wine is forbidden.' Here 'intoxicating' is an essential accident of wine, making the syllogism potentially demonstrative.
The 'illa must be an essential accident for the syllogism to produce certainty.

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the conditions under which premises produce certainty (yaqin) in demonstrative reasoning. The author begins by distinguishing between statements that are necessary in themselves and those that are not. He warns against relying on accepted opinions (maqbulat) and well-known opinions (mashhurat) when seeking certainty, labeling such usage as fallacious. He then addresses a key point: true premises may yield true conclusions, but those conclusions are not automatically certain. They are certain only if the premises are necessary (daruriyya) and appropriate for the desired conclusion.

The author provides an example: the argument 'This human is an animal because it moves, and everything that moves is an animal.' The issue is that if the human stops moving, the knowledge based on movement disappears, so one cannot be certain the human remains an animal. A possible objection is that the major premise 'everything that moves is an animal' is understood as a universal necessary statement: any moving thing is an animal as long as its substance exists. However, the author responds that such a syllogism only yields certainty if it is potentially a demonstrative syllogism (qiyas burhani). The major premise's necessity must be of the kind studied in the book of demonstration (Kitab al-Burhan), not merely in the book of syllogism (Kitab al-Qiyas). In other words, the necessity must be known through its cause ('illa), otherwise the certainty is not real and universal.

The author notes that movement can be an essential accident ('arad dhati) of both human (as an animal) and animal (as a species of body). So if the middle term (movement) is an essential accident, the syllogism is demonstrative in potentiality, even if not in actuality. Certainty arises from this potentiality.

A parallel is then drawn between producing true conclusions from false premises and producing necessary conclusions from non-necessary premises. In both cases, the value of the conclusion does not come from the syllogism's form but from the conclusion's own nature. The author makes four numbered comparisons: (1) Just as false premises can yield true conclusions, non-necessary premises can yield necessary conclusions. (2) The truth/necessity of the conclusion in such cases is due to itself, not the inference. (3) One may doubt the conclusion's truth/necessity even if it is true/necessary in itself, unless its necessity is known from another source. (4) True premises cannot yield a false conclusion, and necessary premises cannot yield a non-necessary conclusion.

The author then clarifies that accidental premises (muqaddimat 'aradiyya) cannot produce a necessary thing (i.e., a necessary conclusion), but they do produce necessarily (i.e., the inference is still valid). He distinguishes between 'producing a necessary thing' (intaj daruri) and 'producing necessarily' (intaj bi-al-darura). Every syllogism produces necessarily, but not every syllogism produces a necessary conclusion. The benefit of a syllogism that produces necessarily without producing a necessary thing is twofold: (1) it gives absolute knowledge (not certain) of the existence of something, and (2) it can be used to refute an opponent who admits the premises. However, the latter is far from demonstration, which depends on the truth itself, not on the opponent's concession. For demonstration, the predicates must be essential (dhati) as previously defined.

Finally, the author states that if the middle term is essential to the minor term, then the syllogism can be demonstrative. This passage is a careful analysis of the epistemic conditions for certainty, emphasizing the need for necessity and essential predication.

Key Terms
ضروري
يقين
برهان
عرضي ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What makes the syllogism 'This human is an animal because it moves, and everything that moves is an animal' potentially demonstrative?
A) The major premise is true and universally quantified.
B) The middle term (movement) is an essential accident.
C) The conclusion is necessary.
D) The opponent admits the minor premise.

Key Takeaway
An essential accident as middle term gives a syllogism potential demonstrative power, yielding certainty even if the syllogism is not actually demonstrative.

Identifying Suitable Predicates for Demonstrative Premises
Types of predicates suitable for demonstrative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of predicates (genus, species, difference, essential accident) are suitable for use in demonstrative premises and give examples from geometry and natural science.

Core Idea
Only essential accidents, not accidental ones, qualify for demonstrative premises.

In demonstrative science, not every predicate is fit for the premises that yield certain knowledge. The author enumerates the kinds of predicates that can serve: the genus of the subject, its species, differences, and essential accidents—but only if they are essential (dhātī) and not accidental (gharīb). This lesson covers these types with concrete examples from geometry and natural science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of suitable predicates
The author enumerates: genus, species, differences, accidents, accidents of accidents, genera of accidents, and their differences. Note the condition 'provided the accidents are essential'.
2
Understand the essential vs accidental distinction
Essential accidents (ʿaraḍ dhātī) are those that necessarily follow from the essence, unlike accidental accidents (ʿaraḍ gharīb).
3
Examine the examples from geometry and natural science
Equality is an essential accident of the genus in geometry. Contrariety is an essential accident of existence in natural science.
4
Apply the condition to mathematical and natural subjects
Mathematical subjects lack contrariety, so contrariety cannot serve as a predicate in mathematical demonstrations.

جنس الموضوع ومن أنواعه وفصوله وأعراضه وأعراض أعراضه وأجناس أعراضه وفصولها وما يجري مجراها بعد أن تكون الأعراض ذاتية لا غريبة. وقد تكون محمولات الصنفين اللذين ذكرناهما من الموضوعات عوارض ذاتية للجنس مثل المساواة في علم الهندسة والعدد، وعوارض ذاتية لما هو شبيه جنس كالمضادة في العلم الطبيعي فإن المضادة من عوارض الموجود الذي هو شبيه جنس. وإنما لا تكون المضادة محمولة في مسائل العلوم الرياضية لأن موضوعات العلم الرياضي إما غير متحركة وإما مشابهة للحركة لا مضادة فيها وإن لم تبق حركاتها من كل جهة. وأما موضوعات العلم الطبيعي فمنتهاه للتغيير بين الأضداد وهي الأجسام الطبيعية من حيث إنها متحركة وساكنة. فأما إذا كان المطلوب هو اللمية دون الإنية فيصلح أن يجعل مقوم ما حدا أوسط يبين به مقوم آخر إذا كان الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر له أو يكون الأكبر أولا للأوسط؛ ولسببه يكون للأصغر كالمدرك فإنه يكون أولا للناطق والحساس ثم للإنسان. فأقول كل ما لم يصلح محمولًا في المسائل البرهانية فلا يصلح أن يكون محمولاً في المقدمات البرهانية البتة خاصة من المبادئ أو عامة إلا الأجناس والفصول وما يشبهها. فإنه يجوز أن تكون محمولة على أنواعها في المقدمات فإنه يمكن أن يكون الأكبر جنسًا للأوسط أو فصلا، والأوسط عرضًا ذاتيا للأصغر. وكما أن العرض يمكن أن يبتدأ فيطلب فكذلك يمكن أن يبتدأ فيطلب جنسه أو فصله، ويمكن أن يكون الأوسط جنسًا للأصغر أو فصلا، والأكبر عرضًا ذاتياً للأوسط: فمن هذه الجهة تدخل الأجناس والفصول في جملة المحمول. وأما أن يكون الأكبر مقوما للأصغر فليس يقع إلا على الوجه المحدود. ولا يقال لما كان من حق الجنس أن لا يحمل على النوع فكيف يعرف وجود النوع في الأصغر ولا يعرف وجود جنسه فإن الجنس ليس مما لا يحمل على النوع وجهًا من وجوه الحمل البتة بل ما لم يخطر معناه بالبال ومعنى النوع بالبال ، ولم تراع النسبة بينهما في هذه الحال أمكن أن يكون غائبا عن الذهن فيمكن أن يخطر النوع بالبال، ولا يلتفت الذهن إلى الجنس فيمكن أن يخطر النوع بالبال محمولا على شيء ولا يخطر الجنس حينئذ. ولا حمله بالفعل بالبال فلا يحمل لكنه إذا أخطر مع النوع بالبال حمل بالفعل على ما يحمل عليه النوع: فإن فرض ذلك الموضوع وحده ولم يلتفت إلى حمل النوع عليه لم يخطر الجنس بالبال البتة، وذلك أولى فإن المُخطر إياه بالبال كان يُخطر ولا يُخطر الجنس بالبال فكيف إذا لم يخطر البتة.
The genus of the subject and its species, differences, accidents, accidents of its accidents, genera of its accidents and their differences, and what takes their place, provided the accidents are essential, not accidental. And there may be predicates of the two categories we mentioned from the subjects: essential accidents of the genus, like equality in the science of geometry and number, and essential accidents of what is like a genus, like contrariety in natural science, for contrariety is an accident of existence which is like a genus. And contrariety is not predicated in the problems of the mathematical sciences because the subjects of mathematical science are either immovable or resemble motion with no contrariety in them, even if their motions are not entirely absent. As for the subjects of natural science, their end is change between opposites, namely natural bodies inasmuch as they are moving and resting. But if the sought is the 'why' rather than the 'that', then it is suitable to make a constitutive [attribute] a middle term to explain another constitutive [attribute], if the middle is a cause for the existence of the greater for it, or the greater is primary for the middle; and because of it it is for the smaller, like 'perceptive' – for it is primarily for 'rational' and 'sensitive', then for 'human'. So I say: everything that is not suitable as a predicate in demonstrative problems is not suitable as a predicate in demonstrative premises at all – excluding the principles or generalities – except genera, differences, and what resembles them. For it is permissible that they be predicated of their types in the premises; for it is possible that the greater is genus or difference for the middle, and the middle is an essential accident for the smaller. And just as an accident can be initially sought, so too can its genus or difference be initially sought, and it is possible that the middle is genus or difference for the smaller, and the greater is an essential accident for the middle: from this aspect, genera and differences enter into the class of predicates. But for the greater to be constitutive of the smaller, that occurs only in the limited way. And do not say: since it is the right of the genus not to be predicated of the species, how can the existence of the species in the smaller be known while the existence of its genus is not known? For the genus is not something that is not predicated of the species in any manner of predication at all; rather, as long as its meaning does not occur to the mind and the meaning of the species does occur to the mind, and the relationship between them is not considered in that state, it is possible that it is absent from the mind. So it is possible that the species occurs to the mind while the mind does not pay attention to the genus, so it is possible that the species occurs to the mind as predicated of something while the genus does not occur then. And it is not actually predicated in the mind, so it is not predicated. But when it occurs together with the species in the mind, it is actually predicated of what the species is predicated of. For if that subject is assumed alone and attention is not paid to the predication of the species upon it, the genus does not occur to the mind at all, and that is more appropriate; for the one who brought it to mind was bringing but not bringing the genus to mind, so how about if it does not occur at all?

Commentary
This is an epistemological passage discussing which predicates are suitable for use in demonstrative premises. The author first enumerates the types of predicates that can serve, including the genus of the subject, its species, differences, and essential accidents. Examples are given from geometry (equality) and natural science (contrariety) to illustrate essential accidents of the genus. The author notes that contrariety is not used in mathematical sciences due to the nature of their subjects. The discussion then turns to the kind of demonstration where the cause ('why') is sought, explaining how a middle term that is a cause can be used. The key principle is stated: unless a predicate is suitable for demonstrative problems, it is not suitable for premises, except for genera and differences. The author explains that genera and differences can be predicated of their species in premises, and provides possible arrangements of the middle term and predicate. Finally, an objection is raised: how can genus be predicated of species if its right is not to be so? The answer clarifies that genus is indeed predicated of species when both concepts are present in the mind; the absence of the mind's attention to the genus does not negate the possibility of predication when both are considered.

Key Takeaway
Only predicates that are essential to the subject—genus, species, difference, and essential accidents—can serve in demonstrative premises.

How Genera and Differences Function in Demonstrative Premises
How genera and differences function as predicates in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how genera and differences can serve as predicates in demonstrative premises and resolve the apparent objection that genus is not predicated of species.

Core Idea
Genus is predicated of species when both concepts are present in the mind.

A subtle point arises: if genus is not predicated of species in ordinary speech, how can it be used as a predicate in a demonstrative premise? The author clarifies that when the mind considers both the genus and the species together, the genus is indeed predicated of the species. This lesson explores that reasoning and also distinguishes between demonstrations of 'that' and 'why'.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection about genus predication
Someone might argue that genus is not predicated of species, e.g., we don't say 'man is animal' in the same way as 'man is rational'. So how can genus be used as a predicate in a premise?
2
Analyze the author's response
The author clarifies that when both genus and species concepts are present in the mind, the genus is indeed predicated. The objection only holds when the mind does not attend to the genus.
3
Apply this to demonstrative premises
In demonstration, we consciously bring both terms to mind, so genus can be used as a predicate of its species in the premise.
4
Distinguish between 'that' and 'why' demonstrations
When the goal is the 'why' (limiyya), a constitutive attribute (muqawwim) can serve as the middle term that explains the presence of the greater term, as in the example of 'perceptive' being primary for 'rational' and 'sensitive'.

Modes of Sharing Principles Among Sciences
Author discusses how sciences share fundamental principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between equal and primary/subsequent modes of sharing principles among Islamic sciences and identify the mode in given examples.

Core Idea
Sciences share principles either equally or in a primary-subsequent order based on subject generality.

In the Islamic intellectual tradition, different sciences often rely on common foundational propositions. Understanding how these principles are shared—whether equally or in a hierarchical manner—helps clarify the relationships between disciplines like fiqh, kalam, and usul al-fiqh. This lesson unpacks the two modes of sharing principles as explained by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes of sharing
The author presents two ways sciences can share principles: either on a single rank (equal) or where the principle belongs primarily to one science and subsequently to another.
Look for the phrases 'على مرتبة واحدة' (single rank) and 'أولاً وللثاني بعده' (primarily and subsequently).
2
Examine the examples given
The first example is the principle of non-contradiction shared by all sciences. The second is the principle 'things equal to the same thing are equal' shared by mathematical sciences. Notice how geometry and optics share this principle: geometry has a more general subject (quantity) than optics (vision), so geometry has it primarily.
Similarly, arithmetic and music: arithmetic's subject (number) is more general than music's (sound), so the principle is primary for arithmetic.
3
Understand the objection and response
A possible objection claims that general principles are either unused or must be specified to each science's subject. The author responds that the intended meaning of sharing is precisely what the examples illustrate: principles are common in a qualified sense, not as completely abstract universals.
This shows that shared principles are not applied identically; they are tailored to the subject matter of each science.
4
Apply the distinction to Islamic sciences
Consider how the principle 'every act is either obligatory or not obligatory' might be shared between usul al-fiqh and fiqh. Which mode applies and why? Reflect on the generality of each science's subject.
This step prepares you for the apply exercise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاشتراك في المبادئ فذلك كاشتراك جميع العلوم في أن الشيء لا يخلو عن الإيجاب والسلب. واشتراك جميع العلوم الرياضية في أن الأشياء المتساوية لشيء واحد متساوية. وتلك الشركة إما أن تكون على مرتبة واحدة كالهندسة والعدد في المبدأ الذي ذكرناه، وإما أن يكون المبدأ لأحدهما أولاً وللثاني بعده مثل أن الهندسة وعلم الناظر يشتركان في هذا المبدأ لكن الهندسة أعم موضوعًا من علم المناظر ؛ فلذلك يكون هذا المبدأ لها أولا وبعدها للمناظر. وكذلك حال الحساب وعلم الموسيقى فإنهما يشتركان في المبدأ الذي ذكره لكن موضوع الحساب أعم من موضوع الموسيقى؛ فيكون هذا المبدأ له قبل أن يكون لعلم الموسيقى. ثم لقائل أن يقول اشتراك العلوم في المبادئ ممتنع لما أن المبادئ العامة غير مستعملة أصلا أو مستعملة مع اختصاصها لموضوع الصناعة. لكنا نقول المعنى من اشتراك المبادئ هو الذي مرَّ ذكره في الأمثلة المذكورة وأمثالها؛
As for sharing in principles, that is like all sciences sharing in that a thing is not devoid of affirmation and negation. And all mathematical sciences share in that things equal to one and the same thing are equal. That sharing is either on a single rank, like geometry and arithmetic in the principle we mentioned; or the principle is for one of them primarily and for the second subsequently, like geometry and optics share in this principle, but geometry is more general in subject than optics, therefore this principle is for it primarily and subsequently for optics. Likewise the case of arithmetic and music: they share in the principle mentioned, but the subject of arithmetic is more general than the subject of music, so this principle belongs to it before it belongs to music. Then someone might say that the sharing of sciences in principles is impossible because general principles are either not used at all or are used with specification to the subject of the art. But we say: the meaning of sharing in principles is what was mentioned in the examples given and the like.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage discusses how sciences can share principles (mabādiʾ), which are fundamental propositions used in demonstrations. The author gives two examples: the principle of non-contradiction (a thing cannot be both affirmed and denied) shared by all sciences, and the principle that things equal to the same thing are equal, shared by all mathematical sciences. He then distinguishes two modes of sharing: either a principle belongs to two sciences equally (like geometry and arithmetic in the equality principle) or it belongs to one science primarily and to another subsequently based on subject generality. For instance, geometry and optics share the equality principle, but since geometry has a more general subject (quantity) than optics (vision), the principle is first for geometry and then for optics. Similarly, arithmetic and music share a principle, but arithmetic's subject (number) is more general than music's subject (sound), so the principle is first for arithmetic. The author then addresses a potential objection: some might claim that shared principles are impossible because general principles are either not used at all or are always specified to the subject of each art. The response is that the intended meaning of sharing is precisely what the examples illustrate—principles are common in a qualified sense, not as completely abstract universals applied identically.

Key Terms
مبادئ
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do geometry and optics share a principle according to the author?
A) They share it equally on a single rank
B) Geometry has it primarily, optics subsequently
C) Optics has it primarily, geometry subsequently
D) They do not share it

Key Takeaway
Principles can be shared among sciences either equally or hierarchically, with the more general science holding the principle primarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the principle 'every event has a cause' (كل حادث له سبب). Identify two Islamic sciences that share this principle (e.g., Kalam and Fiqh). Determine whether the sharing is equal or primary/subsequent, and justify your answer based on subject generality.
?
Choose a pair of Islamic sciences (e.g., Tafsir and Usul al-Tafsir) and propose a principle they share. Explain the mode of sharing and how the subject generality affects it.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
8 min
The Two Sources of Demonstration
Definition of the two sources of demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the two sources of demonstration a given syllogism belongs to based on the essential relations between terms.

Core Idea
Demonstration relies on essential relations; only two patterns yield certain proof.

The author defines the two sources (ma'khadh) that make a syllogism demonstrative. Understanding these sources is the first step to constructing or evaluating a burhan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
Find the major, middle, and minor terms in the syllogism. The middle connects the other two.
2
Check the relation between major and middle
Is the major an essential accident of the middle? If yes, this fits the first source.
3
If not, check the middle-minor relation
Is the middle an essential accident of the minor? Additionally, is the major an essential accident or constituent of the middle? If both hold, this is the second source.
4
Determine demonstrative status
If neither condition holds, the syllogism is not demonstrative. It may be valid but not a burhan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأكبر عرضاً ذاتياً للأوسط فهو المأخذ الأول من مآخذ البرهانيات، أو كان الأكبر عرضاً ذاتياً للأوسط أو مقوماً له؛ والأوسط عرضاً ذاتياً للأصغر وهو المأخذ الثاني من البرهانيات كان النظر في العلمين واحداً. وإن لم يكن كذلك لم يكن القياس برهانياً في كليهما بل عساه أن يكون برهانياً في أحدهما فقط فإن البرهان لا يخلو عن أحد هذين المأخذين.
ثم من المحال أن يبين في أحد المأخذين علمان متباينا الموضوع أو متباينا النظر في الموضوع، ولهذا ليس للمهندس أن يبين هل الأضداد بها علم واحد أو لا؟ فإن الأضداد ليس من جملة موضوعات علمه ولا من العوارض الذاتية له أو لجنسه. ولو كان كذلك لكان من الواجب أن يعلم أنه إنما ينقل البرهان من علم أعلى إلى علم تحته نحو البراهين الهندسية تستعمل في المناظر، والعددية تستعمل في التأليف، ومن الواجب أن لا يتفق بحث علمين متباينين في الموضوعات والأعراض، وأن لا يكون شيء من العلوم ينظر في الأعراض الغريبة ولا في الأعراض التي تعرض للشيء لا بما هو هو مثل الحسن والقبح إذا استعملا في الشكل والخط، والمقابلة إذا روعيت بين المستدير والمستقيم؛ فإن أمثال هذه وإن كانت توجد بوجه ما في موضوعات الهندسية فليست تعرض لها بما هي هي بل هي عوارض غريبة. وقد قيل في التعليم الأول لما كان يجب أن تكون مقدمات البرهان كلية حتى تكون يقينية لا تغير الأمور الشخصية وكذلك النتائج يجب أن تكون كلية ودائمة يجب أن لا يكون برهان على الأمور الجزئية الفاسدة بل على أحوالها قياس ما يدل على أن الأمر هكذا فقط فقولنا زيد حيوان لا يبرهن بالبرهان فإنه إذا فسد لم يكن حيوانا. وكما أن الأمور الجزئية لا برهان عليها فكذلك لا حد لها؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف يجب أن تكون مقدمات البرهان كلية؛ وقد كان من مقدمات البراهين ما هي جزئية، وذلك إذا كانت المطالب جزئية. والبرهان الجزئي وإن لم يكن في شرف البرهان الكلي فإنه بمثله في أنه يعطي اليقين والعلة. لكنا نقول إن الكلي يقال على وجهين: [1] فيقال كلي بالنسبة إلى الشخص المخصوص ويراد به أن الحكم فيه على كل سواء كان على كله أو بعضه أو مهملا، [ب] ويقال كلي بالنسبة إلى الجزئي أو المهمل ويراد به أن الحكم على موضوع كلي وعلى كله؛ والمقدمة الجزئية غير الشخصية فإن موضوعها كلي، والبعض أيضاً كما في قولنا بعض الحيوان ناطق وفيما نحن فيه تدخل فيه المقدمة الجزئية لا الشخصية. ولا يظن أنه متى قام البرهان على الكلي فقد قام على الجزئيات المندرجة تحت ذلك الكلي فإنه وإن قام على الجزئيات فقد قام عليها بالعرض لا بالذات، وكيف وإنها من جملة ما لا يبقى له عقد دائم ولا شيء مما يفيده البرهان كذلك.
ثم لقائل أن يقول أيضاً كيف هو وقد كان من أصحاب العلوم يقيمون البرهان على كثير من الأشياء الواجبة الوقوع المكررة مع أنها من الجزئيات الفاسدة نحو الكسوفات الشمسية والخسوفات القمرية وغيرها. وإذا كان كذلك فقد بطل ما قالوه إن الجزئيات الفاسدة لا برهان عليها ولا حد لها، لكنا نقول: لا نسلم أنهم يقيمون البرهان والحدود على الجزئيات من حيث إنها جزئيات بل إنما يقيمونهما على الكليات، فإن خسوف القمر على الإطلاق نوع ما بذاته مقول على كسوفات قمرية جزئية. وذلك النوع طبيعة معقولة كلية؛ فالبرهان والحد لتلك الطبيعة النوعية ذاتية ودائمة يقينية. وكذلك الكسوف في وقت ما
And the greater term being an essential accident of the middle term is the first source from the sources of demonstrative proofs; or the greater term being an essential accident of the middle term or a constituent of it, and the middle term an essential accident of the lesser term, is the second source of demonstrative proofs, and the consideration in the two sciences is one. And if it is not so, then the syllogism is not demonstrative in both of them, but perhaps it is demonstrative in one of them only, for demonstration is not devoid of one of these two sources.
Then it is impossible that two sciences diverse in subject or diverse in consideration of the subject be explained in one source, and for this the engineer is not to explain whether opposites have one science or not? For opposites are not among the subjects of his science nor among the essential accidents of it or of its genus. And if it were so, it would be necessary to know that demonstration is transferred from a higher science to a science beneath it, such as geometrical proofs used in optics, and arithmetical proofs used in composition, and it is necessary that two sciences diverse in subjects and accidents do not agree in research, and that no science considers accidental accidents nor accidents that occur to a thing not insofar as it is itself, such as beauty and ugliness when used in shape and line, and opposition when considered between curved and straight; for such things, even if they exist in some way in the subjects of geometry, do not occur to them insofar as they are themselves, but are accidental accidents. And it has been said in the First Teaching: Since the premises of demonstration must be universal so that they are certain, not changing with particular matters, and likewise the conclusions must be universal and perpetual, there should be no demonstration of perishable particular things but rather of their states, a syllogism indicating that the matter is thus only. So our statement 'Zaid is an animal' is not demonstrated by demonstration, for when he perishes, he is not an animal. And just as particular things have no demonstration, so they have no definition; that will be known later.
Then one may say: How must the premises of demonstration be universal while some of the premises of demonstrations are particular, that being when the demands are particular? And particular demonstration, even if it is not in the nobility of universal demonstration, is like it in that it gives certainty and cause. But we say that 'universal' is said in two ways: [1] It is said 'universal' relative to the particular individual, meaning that the judgment in it is about all, whether it be about all of it or some of it or unspecified; [b] and it is said 'universal' relative to the particular or unspecified, meaning that the judgment is about a universal subject and about all of it. And the particular premise is not personal, for its subject is universal, and 'some' also, as in our statement 'some animal is rational'. And in what we are discussing, the particular premise enters, not the personal premise. And let one not think that whenever demonstration is established on the universal, it is established on the particulars subsumed under that universal, for even if it is established on the particulars, it is established on them accidentally, not essentially; moreover, they are among that which does not have a permanent bond, nor does anything that demonstration provides [pertain to] them.
Then one may also say: How can it be? For there are practitioners of sciences who establish demonstration on many things that necessarily occur repeatedly, even though they are perishable particulars, such as solar eclipses and lunar eclipses and others. And if that is the case, then their statement that perishable particulars have no demonstration and no definition is invalidated. But we say: We do not concede that they establish demonstration and definitions on particulars insofar as they are particulars; rather, they establish them only on universals. For lunar eclipse absolutely is a certain type in itself, predicated on particular lunar eclipses. And that type is an intelligible universal nature; demonstration and definition are for that specific nature, essential, perpetual, and certain. And likewise for an eclipse at a particular time.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the conditions for demonstrative proof (burhan), specifically the requirement of universality and the two sources of demonstration. The author first defines the two sources: (1) when the major term is an essential accident of the middle term, and (2) when the major term is an essential accident or constituent of the middle, and the middle is an essential accident of the minor. He emphasizes that it is impossible for a single source to explain two sciences with different subjects or considerations, giving the example that an engineer cannot discuss opposites—they are not subjects of his science. He then states that demonstration is transferred from a higher science to a lower one, like geometry to optics. The author insists that premises must be universal and conclusions eternal, so perishable particulars like 'Zaid is an animal' cannot be demonstrated because when he perishes, the statement is false. He addresses an objection that some demonstrations have particular premises when demands are particular. The response distinguishes two meanings of 'universal': one relative to individual persons and another relative to particulars or unspecified terms. A particular premise (e.g., 'some animal is rational') is not personal; its subject is universal. He warns that demonstration on a universal does not automatically apply to its particulars except accidentally, as particulars lack permanent existence. A second objection concerns recurring events like eclipses. The author responds that demonstrations are not on the particular events as particulars, but on the universal type (the nature of lunar eclipse). Thus the universal nature—not the perishable instance—is the proper subject of demonstration and definition. This passage clarifies that burhan is about universal, essential, and eternal matters, not transient particulars.

Key Terms
برهان
مأخذ
عرض ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first source of demonstration?
A) The major is an essential accident of the minor.
B) The major is an essential accident of the middle.
C) The middle is an essential accident of the minor.
D) The major is a constituent of the minor.

Key Takeaway
A demonstrative syllogism must have either the major as essential accident of the middle (first source) or the middle as essential accident of the minor with the major essentially related to the middle (second source).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the argument: 'The world is originated; everything originated has an originator; therefore the world has an originator.' Identify which source it belongs to and verify that the major and middle are related essentially.
?
Construct a syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., riba) that fits the first source.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Why One Demonstration Cannot Serve Two Sciences
Argument against cross-science demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a demonstration from one science cannot be used to prove a conclusion in another science with a different subject.

Core Idea
Each science has its own subject and essential accidents; mixing them violates the sources of demonstration.

The author argues that a single demonstration cannot belong to two different sciences if their subjects differ. This has important implications for how we transfer knowledge between fields.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the first science
What is the primary subject under investigation?
2
Identify the subject of the second science
Do the subjects differ? Are they the same genus?
3
Check if the conclusion involves essential accidents of both
If the demonstration's conclusion pertains to essential accidents of only one subject, it cannot belong to the other science.
4
Determine if transference is valid
Demonstration can only be transferred from a higher science to a subordinate one (e.g., geometry to optics).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأكبر عرضاً ذاتياً للأوسط فهو المأخذ الأول من مآخذ البرهانيات، أو كان الأكبر عرضاً ذاتياً للأوسط أو مقوماً له؛ والأوسط عرضاً ذاتياً للأصغر وهو المأخذ الثاني من البرهانيات كان النظر في العلمين واحداً. وإن لم يكن كذلك لم يكن القياس برهانياً في كليهما بل عساه أن يكون برهانياً في أحدهما فقط فإن البرهان لا يخلو عن أحد هذين المأخذين.

For there are practitioners of sciences who establish demonstration on many things that necessarily occur repeatedly, even though they are perishable particulars, such as solar eclipses and lunar eclipses and others. And if that is the case, then their statement that perishable particulars have no demonstration and no definition is invalidated. But we say: We do not concede that they establish demonstration and definitions on particulars insofar as they are particulars; rather, they establish them only on universals. For lunar eclipse absolutely is a certain type in itself, predicated on particular lunar eclipses. And that type is an intelligible universal nature; demonstration and definition are for that specific nature, essential, perpetual, and certain. And likewise for an eclipse at a particular time.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the conditions for demonstrative proof (burhan), specifically the requirement of universality and the two sources of demonstration. The author first defines the two sources: (1) when the major term is an essential accident of the middle term, and (2) when the major term is an essential accident or constituent of the middle, and the middle is an essential accident of the minor. He emphasizes that it is impossible for a single source to explain two sciences with different subjects or considerations, giving the example that an engineer cannot discuss opposites—they are not subjects of his science. He then states that demonstration is transferred from a higher science to a lower one, like geometry to optics. The author insists that premises must be universal and conclusions eternal, so perishable particulars like 'Zaid is an animal' cannot be demonstrated because when he perishes, the statement is false. He addresses an objection that some demonstrations have particular premises when demands are particular. The response distinguishes two meanings of 'universal': one relative to individual persons and another relative to particulars or unspecified terms. A particular premise (e.g., 'some animal is rational') is not personal; its subject is universal. He warns that demonstration on a universal does not automatically apply to its particulars except accidentally, as particulars lack permanent existence. A second objection concerns recurring events like eclipses. The author responds that demonstrations are not on the particular events as particulars, but on the universal type (the nature of lunar eclipse). Thus the universal nature—not the perishable instance—is the proper subject of demonstration and definition. This passage clarifies that burhan is about universal, essential, and eternal matters, not transient particulars.

Key Terms
متباينة الموضوع
عوارض غريبة
علم أعلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can an engineer not discuss opposites?
A) Because opposites are not subjects of his science.
B) Because engineers only deal with shapes.
C) Because opposites are not essential accidents of his subject.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
A demonstration from one science cannot be applied to another science if their subjects and essential accidents are different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an argument that attempts to prove the existence of Allah from the physical properties of the human body. Is this a valid cross-science demonstration? Why or why not? Use the principle from this lesson.
?
Give an example from your own field where a demonstration from one science is legitimately transferred to a subordinate science (like geometry to optics).

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate
10 min
How Universal Must Demonstrative Premises Be?
Response to an objection about universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two senses of 'universal' and determine whether a premise qualifies as universal for demonstration.

Core Idea
Universal has two senses: one relative to the individual, another relative to the universal subject. Only the latter is required for demonstration.

An objector challenges the requirement that premises must be universal, pointing out that many demonstrations use particular premises. The author responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'universal' and showing that particular premises are not personal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
An objector asks: how can premises be universal if some demonstrations have particular premises?
2
Note the author's response
The author says 'universal' is said in two ways.
3
Distinguish the two meanings
Sense 1: universal relative to a particular individual (e.g., 'all of Zaid is animal'). Sense 2: universal relative to a universal subject (e.g., 'all animals are mortal').
4
Apply to particular premises
A particular premise like 'some animal is rational' has a universal subject ('animal'), so it is not personal. It is acceptable for demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأكبر عرضاً ذاتياً للأوسط فهو المأخذ الأول من مآخذ البرهانيات، أو كان الأكبر عرضاً ذاتياً للأوسط أو مقوماً له؛ والأوسط عرضاً ذاتياً للأصغر وهو المأخذ الثاني من البرهانيات كان النظر في العلمين واحداً. وإن لم يكن كذلك لم يكن القياس برهانياً في كليهما بل عساه أن يكون برهانياً في أحدهما فقط فإن البرهان لا يخلو عن أحد هذين المأخذين.
ثم من المحال أن يبين في أحد المأخذين علمان متباينا الموضوع أو متباينا النظر في الموضوع، ولهذا ليس للمهندس أن يبين هل الأضداد بها علم واحد أو لا؟ فإن الأضداد ليس من جملة موضوعات علمه ولا من العوارض الذاتية له أو لجنسه. ولو كان كذلك لكان من الواجب أن يعلم أنه إنما ينقل البرهان من علم أعلى إلى علم تحته نحو البراهين الهندسية تستعمل في المناظر، والعددية تستعمل في التأليف، ومن الواجب أن لا يتفق بحث علمين متباينين في الموضوعات والأعراض، وأن لا يكون شيء من العلوم ينظر في الأعراض الغريبة ولا في الأعراض التي تعرض للشيء لا بما هو هو مثل الحسن والقبح إذا استعملا في الشكل والخط، والمقابلة إذا روعيت بين المستدير والمستقيم؛ فإن أمثال هذه وإن كانت توجد بوجه ما في موضوعات الهندسية فليست تعرض لها بما هي هي بل هي عوارض غريبة. وقد قيل في التعليم الأول لما كان يجب أن تكون مقدمات البرهان كلية حتى تكون يقينية لا تغير الأمور الشخصية وكذلك النتائج يجب أن تكون كلية ودائمة يجب أن لا يكون برهان على الأمور الجزئية الفاسدة بل على أحوالها قياس ما يدل على أن الأمر هكذا فقط فقولنا زيد حيوان لا يبرهن بالبرهان فإنه إذا فسد لم يكن حيوانا. وكما أن الأمور الجزئية لا برهان عليها فكذلك لا حد لها؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف يجب أن تكون مقدمات البرهان كلية؛ وقد كان من مقدمات البراهين ما هي جزئية، وذلك إذا كانت المطالب جزئية. والبرهان الجزئي وإن لم يكن في شرف البرهان الكلي فإنه بمثله في أنه يعطي اليقين والعلة. لكنا نقول إن الكلي يقال على وجهين: [1] فيقال كلي بالنسبة إلى الشخص المخصوص ويراد به أن الحكم فيه على كل سواء كان على كله أو بعضه أو مهملا، [ب] ويقال كلي بالنسبة إلى الجزئي أو المهمل ويراد به أن الحكم على موضوع كلي وعلى كله؛ والمقدمة الجزئية غير الشخصية فإن موضوعها كلي، والبعض أيضاً كما في قولنا بعض الحيوان ناطق وفيما نحن فيه تدخل فيه المقدمة الجزئية لا الشخصية. ولا يظن أنه متى قام البرهان على الكلي فقد قام على الجزئيات المندرجة تحت ذلك الكلي فإنه وإن قام على الجزئيات فقد قام عليها بالعرض لا بالذات، وكيف وإنها من جملة ما لا يبقى له عقد دائم ولا شيء مما يفيده البرهان كذلك.
ثم لقائل أن يقول أيضاً كيف هو وقد كان من أصحاب العلوم يقيمون البرهان على كثير من الأشياء الواجبة الوقوع المكررة مع أنها من الجزئيات الفاسدة نحو الكسوفات الشمسية والخسوفات القمرية وغيرها. وإذا كان كذلك فقد بطل ما قالوه إن الجزئيات الفاسدة لا برهان عليها ولا حد لها، لكنا نقول: لا نسلم أنهم يقيمون البرهان والحدود على الجزئيات من حيث إنها جزئيات بل إنما يقيمونهما على الكليات، فإن خسوف القمر على الإطلاق نوع ما بذاته مقول على كسوفات قمرية جزئية. وذلك النوع طبيعة معقولة كلية؛ فالبرهان والحد لتلك الطبيعة النوعية ذاتية ودائمة يقينية. وكذلك الكسوف في وقت ما
And the greater term being an essential accident of the middle term is the first source from the sources of demonstrative proofs; or the greater term being an essential accident of the middle term or a constituent of it, and the middle term an essential accident of the lesser term, is the second source of demonstrative proofs, and the consideration in the two sciences is one. And if it is not so, then the syllogism is not demonstrative in both of them, but perhaps it is demonstrative in one of them only, for demonstration is not devoid of one of these two sources.
Then it is impossible that two sciences diverse in subject or diverse in consideration of the subject be explained in one source, and for this the engineer is not to explain whether opposites have one science or not? For opposites are not among the subjects of his science nor among the essential accidents of it or of its genus. And if it were so, it would be necessary to know that demonstration is transferred from a higher science to a science beneath it, such as geometrical proofs used in optics, and arithmetical proofs used in composition, and it is necessary that two sciences diverse in subjects and accidents do not agree in research, and that no science considers accidental accidents nor accidents that occur to a thing not insofar as it is itself, such as beauty and ugliness when used in shape and line, and opposition when considered between curved and straight; for such things, even if they exist in some way in the subjects of geometry, do not occur to them insofar as they are themselves, but are accidental accidents. And it has been said in the First Teaching: Since the premises of demonstration must be universal so that they are certain, not changing with particular matters, and likewise the conclusions must be universal and perpetual, there should be no demonstration of perishable particular things but rather of their states, a syllogism indicating that the matter is thus only. So our statement 'Zaid is an animal' is not demonstrated by demonstration, for when he perishes, he is not an animal. And just as particular things have no demonstration, so they have no definition; that will be known later.
Then one may say: How must the premises of demonstration be universal while some of the premises of demonstrations are particular, that being when the demands are particular? And particular demonstration, even if it is not in the nobility of universal demonstration, is like it in that it gives certainty and cause. But we say that 'universal' is said in two ways: [1] It is said 'universal' relative to the particular individual, meaning that the judgment in it is about all, whether it be about all of it or some of it or unspecified; [b] and it is said 'universal' relative to the particular or unspecified, meaning that the judgment is about a universal subject and about all of it. And the particular premise is not personal, for its subject is universal, and 'some' also, as in our statement 'some animal is rational'. And in what we are discussing, the particular premise enters, not the personal premise. And let one not think that whenever demonstration is established on the universal, it is established on the particulars subsumed under that universal, for even if it is established on the particulars, it is established on them accidentally, not essentially; moreover, they are among that which does not have a permanent bond, nor does anything that demonstration provides [pertain to] them.
Then one may also say: How can it be? For there are practitioners of sciences who establish demonstration on many things that necessarily occur repeatedly, even though they are perishable particulars, such as solar eclipses and lunar eclipses and others. And if that is the case, then their statement that perishable particulars have no demonstration and no definition is invalidated. But we say: We do not concede that they establish demonstration and definitions on particulars insofar as they are particulars; rather, they establish them only on universals. For lunar eclipse absolutely is a certain type in itself, predicated on particular lunar eclipses. And that type is an intelligible universal nature; demonstration and definition are for that specific nature, essential, perpetual, and certain. And likewise for an eclipse at a particular time.

Can Recurring Events Be Demonstrated?
Response to an objection about particular recurring events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how demonstrations about recurring events like eclipses are actually about universal natures, not particular instances.

Core Idea
Even so-called particular events are subsumed under universal types that are the true objects of demonstration.

A second objector points to recurring events like lunar eclipses as examples of demonstrations on particulars. The author responds by arguing that such demonstrations are on the universal nature of the event, not the individual occurrence.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Objector: we have demonstrations of particular recurring events like eclipses, so the claim that only universals can be demonstrated is false.
2
Note the response
The author says they do not demonstrate on the particular as particular, but on the universal type.
3
Identify the universal nature
E.g., 'lunar eclipse absolutely' is a type that applies to all individual eclipses.
4
Understand the implication
The demonstration and definition apply to the type, which is eternal and universal. The particular instance is only an accidental subject.

According to the author, on what is a demonstration of a lunar eclipse truly established?
A) The particular eclipse in a specific year.
B) The universal nature of lunar eclipse.
C) The physical matter of the moon.
D) The time of the eclipse.

Key Takeaway
Demonstrations on recurring events are actually on the universal type, which is eternal and certain, not on the perishable particular.

The Two Senses of 'Universal' in Demonstration
Author defends the claim that particulars lack demonstration and definition against two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two senses of 'universal' used in this passage and determine which sense allows particular premises in demonstration.

Core Idea
'Universal' can mean covering every individual or covering the entire extension of a universal subject.

The objector challenges the claim that demonstration premises must be universal by pointing to demonstrations with particular premises. The author responds by clarifying that 'universal' has two meanings. This distinction is crucial for understanding how particular premises fit into demonstrative science.

Guided Path
1
Locate the first objection
Find the sentence where the objector says premises of demonstration are sometimes particular. It begins with 'ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف يجب أن تكون مقدمات البرهان كلية'.
2
Identify the author's response
Look for the sentence starting with 'لكنا نقول إن الكلي يقال على وجهين'. This introduces the key distinction.
3
Extract the two definitions
Write down the two ways 'universal' is defined: (1) in relation to a specific individual, meaning the judgment applies to every individual (wholly, partially, or indefinitely); (2) in relation to the particular or indefinite, meaning the judgment is on a universal subject covering its entire extension.
4
Apply to the example
Consider the particular premise 'some animal is rational'. Its subject 'animal' is universal, but it does not cover all animals. According to the two senses, this premise is universal in the first sense (judgment about every individual animal? Actually, it only covers some, but the subject is universal). The author says such a premise is not singular and is allowed in demonstration.
The premise has a universal subject, so it is not a singular premise; thus it can be part of a demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا فسد لم يكن حيوانا. وكما أن الأمور الجزئية لا برهان عليها فكذلك لا حد لها؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
ثم لقائل أن يقول كيف يجب أن تكون مقدمات البرهان كلية؛ وقد كان من مقدمات البراهين ما هي جزئية، وذلك إذا كانت المطالب جزئية. والبرهان الجزئي وإن لم يكن في شرف البرهان الكلي فإنه بمثله في أنه يعطي اليقين والعلة. لكنا نقول إن الكلي يقال على وجهين : [1] فيقال كلي بالنسبة إلى الشخص المخصوص ويراد به أن الحكم فيه على كل سواء كان على كله أو بعضه أو مهملا ، [ب] ويقال كلي بالنسبة إلى الجزئي أو المهمل ويراد به أن الحكم على موضوع كلي وعلى كله؛ والمقدمة الجزئية غير الشخصية فإن موضوعها كلي، والبعض أيضًا كما في قولنا بعض الحيوان ناطق وفيما نحن فيه تدخل فيه المقدمة الجزئية لا الشخصية. ولا يظن أنه متى قام البرهان على الكلي فقد قام على الجزئيات المندرجة تحت ذلك الكلي فإنه وإن قام على الجزئيات فقد قام عليها بالعرض لا بالذات، وكيف وإنها من جملة ما لا يبقى له عقد دائم ولا شيء مما يفيده البرهان كذلك. ثم لقائل أن يقول أيضًا كيف هو وقد كان من أصحاب العلوم يقيمون البرهان على كثير من الأشياء الواجبة الوقوع المكرَّرة مع أنها من الجزئيات الفاسدة نحو الكسوفات الشمسية والخسوفات القمرية وغيرها. وإذا كان كذلك فقد بطل ما قالوه إن الجزئيات الفاسدة لا برهان عليها ولا حد لها، لكنا نقول: لا نسلم أنهم يقيمون البرهان والحدود على الجزئيات من حيث إنها جزئيات بل إنما يقيمونهما على الكليات، فإن خسوف القمر على الإطلاق نوع ما بذاته مقول على كسوفات قمرية جزئية. وذلك النوع طبيعة معقولة كلية؛ فالبرهان والحد لتلك الطبيعة النوعية ذاتية ودائمة يقينية. وكذلك الكسوف في وقت ما فإنه وإن اتفق أن لا يكون إلا واحدا فليس نفس تصوره كسوفًا قمريًا في وقت حالة كذا يمنع أن يكون مقولاً على كثيرين. وكذلك الكلام في الحدود فإنها للكليات لا للجزئيات. ثمَّ الشيء وإن كان يجب أن يكون واحدًا بالبراهين الخارجية فذلك لا يخرجه عن كونه كليا، ألا ترى أن الشمس واحد والقمر واحد والعالم واحد مع أن الكل كلي.
If it perishes, it is no longer an animal. And just as there is no demonstration of particular things, so too there is no definition of them; that will be known later. Then one may say: how must the premises of demonstration be universal, when among the premises of demonstrations there are particular ones, that is when the demands are particular. And the particular demonstration, though not in the honor of the universal demonstration, is like it in that it gives certainty and the cause. But we say: the universal is said in two ways: [1] it is said universal in relation to the specific individual, meaning that the judgment in it applies to every (individual), whether wholly or partially or indefinitely; [2] and it is said universal in relation to the particular or the indefinite, meaning that the judgment is on a universal subject and on its entirety. And the particular premise is not singular, for its subject is universal, and 'some' as well, as in our saying 'some animal is rational'. And in our case, the particular premise enters, not the singular one. And let one not think that whenever demonstration stands on the universal, it has stood on the particulars subsumed under that universal; for even if it stands on the particulars, it stands on them accidentally, not essentially. How could it, since they are among things that have no permanent constitution, and nothing that demonstration bestows is like that? Then one may also say: how is it, when practitioners of sciences have established demonstration on many things that necessarily occur repeatedly, even though they are among perishable particulars, such as solar eclipses, lunar eclipses, and others? If that is so, then what they said—that perishable particulars have no demonstration or definition—is invalid. But we say: we do not grant that they establish demonstration and definitions on particulars insofar as they are particulars; rather they establish them on universals. For lunar eclipse absolutely is a species in itself, predicated of particular lunar eclipses. And that species is an intelligible universal nature; so the demonstration and definition are for that specific nature, essential, permanent, and certain. Likewise, an eclipse at a particular time, even if it happens to be only one, its very conception as a lunar eclipse at a time and state of such-and-such does not prevent it being predicated of many. And similarly the discourse on definitions: they are for universals, not for particulars. Moreover, even if a thing must be one by external proofs, that does not remove it from being universal. Do you not see that the sun is one, the moon is one, and the world is one, yet all are universal?
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Aristotelian doctrine that demonstration and definition apply only to universals, not to perishable particulars. The author begins by affirming that if a particular thing perishes, it ceases to be that thing (e.g., an animal that dies is no longer an animal). Therefore, there is no demonstration or definition of such particulars. Two objections are then raised. The first objection: premises of demonstration must be universal, yet some demonstrations use particular premises (e.g., when the conclusion is particular). The author responds by distinguishing two senses of 'universal'. In the first sense, 'universal' refers to a judgment covering every individual (the whole, part, or indefinite). In the second sense, it refers to a judgment on a universal subject covering its entire extension. A particular premise (e.g., 'some animal is rational') is not a singular premise (e.g., 'this animal is rational') because its subject is still a universal. Such particular premises are allowed in demonstrations of particular conclusions. The author then warns against assuming that a demonstration of a universal automatically covers its particulars essentially; it covers them only accidentally, because particulars are perishable and do not have the permanent nature that demonstration addresses. The second objection gives examples like eclipses, which are regularly recurring particulars. The author replies that the demonstration is actually on the universal nature (e.g., lunar eclipse absolutely) which is a species predicated of particular instances. That species is a universal intelligible nature; the demonstration and definition apply to it essentially, not to the particular instances as such. Even a unique event (like a particular eclipse) can be conceived as a universal in terms of its kind. Similarly, definitions are for universals. Finally, the author notes that even if external proofs show a thing to be one individual (e.g., the sun), that does not prevent it from being conceived universally in the mind. Thus the original doctrine is upheld.

Key Terms
برهان
جزئي
كلي
مقدمة جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the subject of a particular premise like 'some animal is rational'?
A) A singular individual.
B) A universal term.
C) An indefinite term.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
A particular premise is not singular; its subject is a universal term, so it can still be part of a demonstrative syllogism.

?
Consider the fiqh principle 'النار لا تحل الميتة' (fire does not make carrion permissible). Is this a universal judgment in the first sense or second sense? Explain.
?

Why Demonstration of Universals Does Not Essentially Cover Particulars
Author defends the claim that particulars lack demonstration and definition against two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a demonstration of a universal applies to its particulars only accidentally, not essentially.

Core Idea
Particulars are perishable, so demonstration, which gives permanent knowledge, cannot essentially apply to them.

After addressing the first objection, the author adds an important caveat: even when a demonstration is about a universal, it does not truly cover the particular instances of that universal in an essential way. This is because particulars come and go, while demonstration is about permanent essences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the caveat
Find the sentence that begins 'ولا يظن أنه متى قام البرهان على الكلي فقد قام على الجزئيات'.
2
Identify the distinction
The author distinguishes between applying to particulars 'essentially' (بالذات) and 'accidentally' (بالعرض).
3
Understand the reason
The author gives a reason: particulars are among things that have no permanent constitution (لا يبقى له عقد دائم).
4
Reflect on the implication
This means that when you prove something about the universal 'human', it does not really prove it about Socrates as an individual, because Socrates will die. The demonstration is essentially about the concept, not the individual.
