The Eclipse Objection and the Universal Nature of Recurring Events
Author defends the claim that particulars lack demonstration and definition against two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue, using the author's reasoning, that demonstrations of recurring natural events (like eclipses) are actually about universal species, not particular occurrences.

Core Idea
Even unique events can be conceived universally; demonstration targets the universal intelligible nature.

The second objection seems powerful: scientists demonstrate eclipses, which are recurring particulars. But the author replies that the demonstration is on the universal species 'lunar eclipse', not on each individual eclipse. This shows how the doctrine can accommodate scientific practice.

Guided Path
1
Locate the second objection
Find the sentence starting with 'ثم لقائل أن يقول أيضًا كيف هو'.
2
Identify the example
The objector gives solar and lunar eclipses as examples of perishable particulars that are demonstrated.
3
Find the author's response
Read from 'لكنا نقول' to the end of the passage.
4
Extract the key move
The author says demonstration is on the universal nature 'lunar eclipse absolutely' (خسوف القمر على الإطلاق), which is a species predicated of particular eclipses.
5
Understand the further argument
Even a unique event can be universal in concept, as the sun is one but universal. The author also explicitly says definitions are for universals, not particulars.

And in our case, the particular premise enters, not the singular one. And let one not think that whenever demonstration stands on the universal, it has stood on the particulars subsumed under that universal; for even if it stands on the particulars, it stands on them accidentally, not essentially. How could it, since they are among things that have no permanent constitution, and nothing that demonstration bestows is like that? Then one may also say: how is it, when practitioners of sciences have established demonstration on many things that necessarily occur repeatedly, even though they are among perishable particulars, such as solar eclipses, lunar eclipses, and others? If that is so, then what they said—that perishable particulars have no demonstration or definition—is invalid. But we say: we do not grant that they establish demonstration and definitions on particulars insofar as they are particulars; rather they establish them on universals. For lunar eclipse absolutely is a species in itself, predicated of particular lunar eclipses. And that species is an intelligible universal nature; so the demonstration and definition are for that specific nature, essential, permanent, and certain. Likewise, an eclipse at a particular time, even if it happens to be only one, its very conception as a lunar eclipse at a time and state of such-and-such does not prevent it being predicated of many. And similarly the discourse on definitions: they are for universals, not for particulars. Moreover, even if a thing must be one by external proofs, that does not remove it from being universal. Do you not see that the sun is one, the moon is one, and the world is one, yet all are universal?
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Aristotelian doctrine that demonstration and definition apply only to universals, not to perishable particulars. The author begins by affirming that if a particular thing perishes, it ceases to be that thing (e.g., an animal that dies is no longer an animal). Therefore, there is no demonstration or definition of such particulars. Two objections are then raised. The first objection: premises of demonstration must be universal, yet some demonstrations use particular premises (e.g., when the conclusion is particular). The author responds by distinguishing two senses of 'universal'. In the first sense, 'universal' refers to a judgment covering every individual (the whole, part, or indefinite). In the second sense, it refers to a judgment on a universal subject covering its entire extension. A particular premise (e.g., 'some animal is rational') is not a singular premise (e.g., 'this animal is rational') because its subject is still a universal. Such particular premises are allowed in demonstrations of particular conclusions. The author then warns against assuming that a demonstration of a universal automatically covers its particulars essentially; it covers them only accidentally, because particulars are perishable and do not have the permanent nature that demonstration addresses. The second objection gives examples like eclipses, which are regularly recurring particulars. The author replies that the demonstration is actually on the universal nature (e.g., lunar eclipse absolutely) which is a species predicated of particular instances. That species is a universal intelligible nature; the demonstration and definition apply to it essentially, not to the particular instances as such. Even a unique event (like a particular eclipse) can be conceived as a universal in terms of its kind. Similarly, definitions are for universals. Finally, the author notes that even if external proofs show a thing to be one individual (e.g., the sun), that does not prevent it from being conceived universally in the mind. Thus the original doctrine is upheld.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Appropriateness and Essential Middle Term in Demonstration
Author explains conditions for appropriate demonstration and the essential middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess whether a middle term in a syllogism is appropriate for demonstration by checking if it is an essential attribute of the subject.

Core Idea
The middle term must be an essential accident or predicate to reveal the cause.

In this passage, the author builds on Aristotle's Posterior Analytics to add a crucial condition for demonstrative premises: they must be 'appropriate' (munasaba) to the subject. The heart of appropriateness is that the middle term must be drawn from the essential attributes (al-'awarid al-dhatiyya) of the subject. This ensures that the demonstration gives the reason why, not just that something is the case. The author illustrates this with geometric and musical examples, and explains how higher sciences provide the reasoned fact (burhan lim) for lower sciences, which give only the fact (burhan inn).

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for demonstration
The author lists four conditions from the 'First Teaching': premises must be true, primary, said-of-all, and appropriate. Focus on the last condition, which is the main topic.
The term 'appropriate' (munasaba) is key.
2
Extract the requirement for the middle term
The passage states: 'the middle term must be from the essential accidents and essential predicates.' This means the middle term must be an attribute that necessarily belongs to the subject due to its essence.
Essential accidents are attributes like 'having three sides' for a triangle, not accidental like 'being drawn in ink'.
3
Apply the condition to the triangle example
The author gives an example: to prove that the angles of a triangle sum to two right angles, the middle term must be from the essential attributes of the triangle (e.g., being a figure with three sides) or of its genus (plane figure). This ensures the proof is demonstrative.
If the middle term were from an unrelated genus, the syllogism would not be appropriate.
4
Understand the transfer between sciences
When the middle term comes from a higher genus, the demonstration is transferred from a higher science to a lower one. For instance, geometry (higher) provides the cause for optics (lower), and arithmetic for music.
This is why the lower science gives burhan inn (fact) and the higher gives burhan lim (reasoned fact).

Source Text

قيل في التعليم الأول: إنه يجب أن لا يقتصر في إقامة البرهان على أن تكون المقدمات صادقة فقط بل يجب مع ذلك أن تكون أولية غير ذات أوساط، ولا على أن يكون مع ذلك مقولة على الكل فقط بل يجب مع ذلك كله أن تكون مناسبة على ما أشرنا إليه غير مرة . والمناسبة من اللوازم برهانية كانت الأقيسة أو جدلية. وقد قيل في التعليم الأول أيضًا يجب أن يكون الحد الأوسط من العوارض الذاتية والمحمولات الذاتية حتى يكون البرهان مناسبا » (۳) ويكون إنما قام البرهان على الشيء من جهة ما هو مثلا لو أردنا أن نبين أن زوايا المثلث مساوية لقائمتين فيجب أن نأخذ الحد الأوسط من الأمور الذاتية للمثلث أو لجنس المثلث؛ وبالجملة للموضوع الذي المثلث من عوارضه الذاتية. فإن كان أوسط من جنس آخر فيجب أن يكون من جنس أعلى؛ وينقل عنه إلى ما تحته كما بينا من حال الهندسة والمناظر، والحساب والموسيقى. ويكون السبب في ذلك هو المشاركة في الموضوع بوجه ما على ما قيل من قبل فيكون حينئذ العلم الأسفل يعطي برهان إن والعلم الأعلى
Chapter: On the realization of the appropriateness of demonstrative and dialectical [premises] to their demands and how the two sciences differ in giving the 'why' and the 'what'.
It was said in the First Teaching: It is necessary that in establishing a demonstration one does not restrict [the premises] to being true only, but also they must be primary and without middle terms; nor only that they are said-of-all, but rather with all that they must be appropriate, as we have indicated more than once. And appropriateness is among the necessities, whether the syllogisms are demonstrative or dialectical. And it was also said in the First Teaching that the middle term must be from the essential accidents and essential predicates so that the demonstration is appropriate. And the demonstration is established on the thing insofar as it is that thing. For example, if we wish to prove that the angles of a triangle are equal to two right angles, we must take the middle term from the essential attributes of the triangle or of the genus of the triangle; in short, from the subject of which the triangle is an essential accident. If the middle term is from another genus, it must be from a higher genus; and it is transferred from it to what is below it, as we have explained regarding the case of geometry and optics, and arithmetic and music. And the reason for that is the sharing in the subject in some way, as was said before. Then in that case the lower science gives a demonstration of the fact (burhan inn) and the higher science [gives a demonstration of the reasoned fact].
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the conditions that make a syllogism appropriate (munasib) for demonstration or dialectic. The author cites the 'First Teaching' (Aristotle's Posterior Analytics) to state that demonstrative premises must be not only true but also primary (i.e., not requiring further proof) and 'said-of-all' (universal), and additionally, they must be appropriate to the subject matter. Appropriateness is a necessary condition for both demonstrative and dialectical syllogisms. Specifically, the middle term must be from the essential attributes (al-'awarid al-dhatiyya) and essential predicates of the subject. This ensures that the demonstration reveals the cause. An example is given: to prove that the angles of a triangle sum to two right angles, the middle term must be something essential to the triangle (like its being a figure with three sides) or to its genus (like plane figure). If the middle term belongs to a different genus, it must be from a higher genus that encompasses the subject, and then the demonstration is transferred from the higher science to the lower one. This is illustrated by the relationship between geometry and optics (optics uses geometry to explain vision) and arithmetic and music (music uses arithmetic ratios). The reason for this transfer is that the subjects share in some way. Consequently, the lower science provides a demonstration of the fact (burhan inn, that something is the case) while the higher science provides the demonstration of the reasoned fact (burhan lim, why it is the case). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the principle is clear.

عوارض ذاتية
حد أوسط
برهان إن
برهان لم

B) It must be an essential accident or essential predicate of the subject.
C) It must be from a different genus.
D) It must be taken from the lower science only.

Key Takeaway
A demonstrative syllogism is only appropriate when its middle term is an essential attribute of the subject, enabling it to provide the reasoned fact (burhan lim) rather than just the fact (burhan inn).

Demonstration -- Burhan

Conditions for a Proper Demonstration
Conditions for proper demonstration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given syllogism qualifies as a proper demonstration by checking if the middle term is an essential attribute of the subject.

Core Idea
A demonstration is more than a valid syllogism with true premises; its middle term must be essential to the subject.

In this passage, the author builds on the Aristotelian tradition to specify that for a demonstration to yield certain knowledge, the middle term must be from the essential attributes (al-'awarid al-dhatiyya) of the subject. We will unpack what that means and how to apply it to real Islamic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate of the conclusion
Find the thing being proved (e.g., triangle) and the property attributed to it (e.g., angle sum equals two right angles).
2
Determine the middle term
What intermediate concept connects subject and predicate? For the triangle example, a possible middle term is 'being a polygon inscribed in a semicircle'. The author insists it must be from the triangle's essence.
3
Check if the middle term is an essential attribute
Ask: does this property belong to the subject by virtue of its definition? For 'triangle', 'having three sides' is essential; 'being drawn by a ruler' is accidental.
4
Verify premises are appropriate and primary
Ensure premises are self-evident or derived from higher principles, not in need of further proof.

Chapter: On clarifying the appropriateness of demonstrative and dialectical [arguments] for their demands and how the two sciences differ in giving 'God' and 'Now'. It was said in the First Teaching: One should not restrict the establishment of demonstration to premises being true only, but they must also be primary and without middle terms; nor only that they be predicated of all, but they must also be appropriate as we have indicated many times. And appropriateness is among the necessary concomitants, whether the syllogisms are demonstrative or dialectical. It was also said in the First Teaching: The middle term must be from essential attributes and essential predications so that the demonstration is appropriate and the demonstration is established about the thing in respect of what it is. For example, if we want to prove that the angles of a triangle equal two right angles, we must take the middle term from the essential properties of the triangle or of the genus of the triangle; in short, from the subject of which the triangle is an essential attribute. If the middle term is from another genus, it must be from a higher genus, and it is transferred from it to what is below it, as we have explained concerning the case of geometry and optics, arithmetic and music. The reason for this is the sharing in the subject in some respect, as was said before. Then the lower science gives a proof of fact (burhan 'inn) and the higher science gives a proof of reason (burhan limma). That is because the premises in the lower science are taken as accepted on the way of subjects or postulates whose causes are unknown; and it is known that their results are not certain unless certainty is obtained in their premises. Certainty in their premises is obtained only in the higher science, since the middle term is essentially in the higher science. If that demonstration is transferred to the lower science, then that which exists in the lower science is not from it. As for the difference in the assistance of the two sciences regarding proof of reason and proof of fact: We say—as for two sciences differing in superiority and non-superiority, most often the demonstration giving the reason from the higher to the lower is completed by the higher giving to the lower premises that are the principles of the demonstration. Also of this type is that one of them gives a demonstration whose middle term is a cause, and the other gives another demonstration whose middle term is another cause prior to that cause, which is the cause of the cause; so the lower does not give the cause completely. For example: Natural science and first philosophy share in the investigation of the similarity and difference of the first motion, but natural science gives the cause which is the natural cause that has no opposite, and the simple matter that has no difference, so corruption or change cannot occur. And first philosophy gives the separate efficient cause which is pure good and pure intellect, and the first universal cause which is pure existence. The demonstration in the two sciences is different, but natural science, even though it gives some demonstration, does not give the proof of reason (burhan limma) absolutely; rather, it gives that it is similar as long as matter exists and that nature exists. The higher science gives the proof of reason that is perpetual absolutely, and gives the cause of the perpetuity of matter and the nature that has no opposite, so its consequence is perpetual. This is for the most part concerning the assistance of the higher science in proof of reason. As for in the minority [of cases], perhaps the higher science takes premises of proof of reason from the lower science, provided that those premises do not depend for their correctness on the correctness of principles that are only explained in the higher science or...
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the requirements for a proper demonstration (burhan) and the distinction between proof of fact (burhan 'inn) and proof of reason (burhan limma). It opens by citing the 'First Teaching' (Aristotle's Posterior Analytics) regarding the conditions for demonstration: premises must be true, primary (awwaliyya), immediate (without middle terms), and appropriately related to the subject. The middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) must be from the essential attributes (al-'awarid al-dhatiyya) of the subject, ensuring the demonstration shows why the attribute holds. For example, to prove that the angles of a triangle equal two right angles, the middle term must be an essential property of the triangle or its genus. The author then relates this to the hierarchy of sciences: a lower science (e.g., optics) gives a proof of fact (that something is the case), while a higher science (e.g., geometry) gives a proof of reason (why it is so). The lower science accepts its premises as given, without knowing their causes; certainty is achieved only when the premises are grounded in the higher science, where the middle term is essential. The passage further illustrates with examples from natural science and metaphysics. Natural science explains motion by natural causes and matter, but metaphysics gives the ultimate cause—the separate efficient cause (pure good, pure intellect) and universal existence. Thus, natural science provides only a partial proof, not a full proof of reason. The passage also notes an exceptional case where the higher science might take some premises from the lower science, but this is rare. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes the epistemic hierarchy and the role of essential attributes in achieving certainty.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Burhan 'inn vs Burhan limma and the Hierarchy of Sciences
Distinction between proof of fact and proof of reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a proof of fact (burhan 'inn) and a proof of reason (burhan limma) and explain how higher and lower sciences provide different types of demonstration.

Core Idea
The lower science tells us that something is true; the higher science tells us why it is true.

The author explains that when two sciences share a subject, the lower science provides a demonstration that establishes the fact (burhan 'inn) while the higher science provides the demonstration that reveals the cause (burhan limma). This epistemic hierarchy ensures certainty. We will explore this distinction and see how it applies to real Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sciences
Find a pair of sciences where one is considered higher (more general, more fundamental) and the other lower (more specific, applied). Example: geometry (higher) and optics (lower) from the passage.
2
Determine the subject they share
What phenomenon or thing do both sciences study? In the passage, optics studies visual phenomena, geometry studies spatial properties; they share the subject of light rays or figures.
3
Compare the middle terms used
In the lower science, the middle term is taken from the specific subject and its premises are accepted without causal explanation. In the higher science, the middle term is an essential attribute from a higher genus, providing the cause.
4
Classify the type of proof
If the demonstration establishes the existence or truth without giving the cause, it is burhan 'inn. If it gives the cause (why it is true), it is burhan limma.

لقائمتين فيجب أن نأخذ الحد الأوسط من الأمور الذاتية للمثلث أو لجنس المثلث؛ وبالجملة للموضوع الذي المثلث من عوارضه الذاتية. فإن كان أوسط من جنس آخر فيجب أن يكون من جنس أعلى؛ وينقل عنه إلى ما تحته كما بينا من حال الهندسة والمناظر، والحساب والموسيقى. ويكون السبب في ذلك هو المشاركة في الموضوع بوجه ما على ما قيل من قبل فيكون حينئذ العلم الأسفل يعطي برهان إن والعلم الأعلى يعطي برهان لم. وذلك لأن المقدّمات تكون في العلم الأسفل مأخوذة مسلمة على سبيل موضوعات أو مصادرات غير معلومة العلل؛ ومعلوم أن نتائجها لا تكون يقينية ما لم يحصل اليقين بمقدماتها. وإنما يحصل اليقين بمقدماتها في العلم الأعلى؛ إذ الأوسط إنما هو بالذات في العلم الأعلى، فإن نقل ذلك البرهان إلى العلم الأسفل كان ذلك في العلم الأسفل ما ليس منه. وأما اختلاف معونة العلمين على اللم والإن؛ فنقول أما العلمان المختلفان في العلو واللا علوّ ، ففي الأكثر إنما يتم البرهان المعطي للم من العلم الأعلى للأسفل بأن يعطي الأعلى للأسفل مقدمات توجد مبادئ البرهان. ومن هذا القبيل أيضًا أن يكون أحدهما يعطي برهانا حده الأوسط علة ما، والثاني يعطي برهانا آخر حده الأوسط علة ما أخرى قبل تلك العلة؛ وهي علة العلة فيكون الاسفل لم يعط العلة بالتمام، مثاله أن العلم الطبيعي والفلسفة الأولى يشتركان في النظر في تشابه الحركة الأولى وتباينها لكن العلم الطبيعي يعطي العلة التي هي الطبيعية التي لا ضد لها والمادة البسيطة التي لا اختلاف فيها؛ فيمتنع أن يعرض فساد أو تغير. والفلسفة الأولى تعطي الفاعلية المفارقة التي هي الخير المحـض والعقل المحض والعلة العامة المقابلة الأولى هي الوجود المحض. والبرهان في العلمين مختلفان لكن العلم الطبيعي مع أنه يعطي برهانا ما فإنه لم يعطِ «البرهان اللّمّي» مطلقا بل يعطي أن ذلك متشابه ما دامت المادة موجودة؛ وتلك الطبيعة موجودة، والعلم الأعلى يعطي البرهان واللّمّي الدائم مطلقا، ويعطي علة دوام المادة؛ والطبيعة التي لا ضد لها فيدوم مقتضاها فهذا ما هو على الأكثر من حال معونة الأعلى في «اللّم». وأما في الأقل فربَّما أخذ العلم الأعلى مبادئ اللم من العلم الأسفل بعد أن لا تكون تلك المبادئ متوقعة في الصحة على صحة مبادٍ إنما يبين في العلم الأعلى أو
Chapter: On clarifying the appropriateness of demonstrative and dialectical [arguments] for their demands and how the two sciences differ in giving 'God' and 'Now'. It was said in the First Teaching: One should not restrict the establishment of demonstration to premises being true only, but they must also be primary and without middle terms; nor only that they be predicated of all, but they must also be appropriate as we have indicated many times. And appropriateness is among the necessary concomitants, whether the syllogisms are demonstrative or dialectical. It was also said in the First Teaching: The middle term must be from essential attributes and essential predications so that the demonstration is appropriate and the demonstration is established about the thing in respect of what it is. For example, if we want to prove that the angles of a triangle equal two right angles, we must take the middle term from the essential properties of the triangle or of the genus of the triangle; in short, from the subject of which the triangle is an essential attribute. If the middle term is from another genus, it must be from a higher genus, and it is transferred from it to what is below it, as we have explained concerning the case of geometry and optics, arithmetic and music. The reason for this is the sharing in the subject in some respect, as was said before. Then the lower science gives a proof of fact (burhan 'inn) and the higher science gives a proof of reason (burhan limma). That is because the premises in the lower science are taken as accepted on the way of subjects or postulates whose causes are unknown; and it is known that their results are not certain unless certainty is obtained in their premises. Certainty in their premises is obtained only in the higher science, since the middle term is essentially in the higher science. If that demonstration is transferred to the lower science, then that which exists in the lower science is not from it. As for the difference in the assistance of the two sciences regarding proof of reason and proof of fact: We say—as for two sciences differing in superiority and non-superiority, most often the demonstration giving the reason from the higher to the lower is completed by the higher giving to the lower premises that are the principles of the demonstration. Also of this type is that one of them gives a demonstration whose middle term is a cause, and the other gives another demonstration whose middle term is another cause prior to that cause, which is the cause of the cause; so the lower does not give the cause completely. For example: Natural science and first philosophy share in the investigation of the similarity and difference of the first motion, but natural science gives the cause which is the natural cause that has no opposite, and the simple matter that has no difference, so corruption or change cannot occur. And first philosophy gives the separate efficient cause which is pure good and pure intellect, and the first universal cause which is pure existence. The demonstration in the two sciences is different, but natural science, even though it gives some demonstration, does not give the proof of reason (burhan limma) absolutely; rather, it gives that it is similar as long as matter exists and that nature exists. The higher science gives the proof of reason that is perpetual absolutely, and gives the cause of the perpetuity of matter and the nature that has no opposite, so its consequence is perpetual. This is for the most part concerning the assistance of the higher science in proof of reason. As for in the minority [of cases], perhaps the higher science takes premises of proof of reason from the lower science, provided that those premises do not depend for their correctness on the correctness of principles that are only explained in the higher science or...
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the requirements for a proper demonstration (burhan) and the distinction between proof of fact (burhan 'inn) and proof of reason (burhan limma). It opens by citing the 'First Teaching' (Aristotle's Posterior Analytics) regarding the conditions for demonstration: premises must be true, primary (awwaliyya), immediate (without middle terms), and appropriately related to the subject. The middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) must be from the essential attributes (al-'awarid al-dhatiyya) of the subject, ensuring the demonstration shows why the attribute holds. For example, to prove that the angles of a triangle equal two right angles, the middle term must be an essential property of the triangle or its genus. The author then relates this to the hierarchy of sciences: a lower science (e.g., optics) gives a proof of fact (that something is the case), while a higher science (e.g., geometry) gives a proof of reason (why it is so). The lower science accepts its premises as given, without knowing their causes; certainty is achieved only when the premises are grounded in the higher science, where the middle term is essential. The passage further illustrates with examples from natural science and metaphysics. Natural science explains motion by natural causes and matter, but metaphysics gives the ultimate cause—the separate efficient cause (pure good, pure intellect) and universal existence. Thus, natural science provides only a partial proof, not a full proof of reason. The passage also notes an exceptional case where the higher science might take some premises from the lower science, but this is rare. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes the epistemic hierarchy and the role of essential attributes in achieving certainty.

Key Terms
برهان إنّ
برهان لمّ
عوارض ذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of demonstration does the lower science typically provide?
A) Proof of reason (burhan limma).
B) Proof of fact (burhan 'inn).
C) Both equally.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The epistemic hierarchy of sciences is reflected in the types of demonstration: lower sciences give burhan 'inn (that it is), higher sciences give burhan limma (why it is).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the example from the passage: optics proves that the image in a mirror is reversed (burhan 'inn), while geometry explains why based on the laws of reflection (burhan limma). Identify the higher and lower science and the type of demonstration.
?
Consider the statement: 'Smoke indicates fire.' Express this as a burhan 'inn and as a burhan limma using an Islamic context, e.g., smoke from a stove.

Demonstration -- Burhan
advanced

Natural Science and Metaphysics: An Example of Burhan limma
Natural science and metaphysics example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the demonstration types used in natural science and metaphysics regarding the first motion and explain what each science contributes to a full burhan limma.

Core Idea
Natural science gives a proximate cause; metaphysics gives the ultimate cause, completing the burhan limma.

The author provides a concrete illustration: natural science and metaphysics both study the first motion. Natural science gives a partial cause (nature and matter), but metaphysics gives the full cause (the separate efficient cause). This shows how burhan limma is achieved at the highest level. We will unpack this example to see how the hierarchy of demonstration works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon under study
The first motion (al-haraka al-ula) and its similarity and difference. Both natural science and metaphysics investigate why it is similar (unchanging) or different.
2
Extract natural science's explanation
Natural science gives the cause as 'the nature that has no opposite' and 'simple matter that has no difference'. These prevent corruption or change. This is a proximate cause.
3
Extract metaphysics' explanation
Metaphysics gives the cause as 'the separate efficient cause' (pure good, pure intellect) and 'the first universal cause' (pure existence). This is the ultimate cause.
4
Classify the type of demonstration each provides
Natural science gives a demonstration that establishes similarity but does not give the absolute burhan limma because it lacks the ultimate cause. Metaphysics gives the full burhan limma, perpetual and absolute.
The passage says natural science 'does not give the burhan limma absolutely' but gives a partial demonstration.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Cooperation of Higher and Lower Sciences in Demonstration
Explains how higher and lower sciences provide limmi and inni demonstrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between limmi and inni demonstrations in the context of scientific cooperation and identify which science provides which type to a given fact.

Core Idea
The higher science gives the cause; the lower science often gives the fact.

The passage explores how different sciences collaborate to produce certain knowledge. The higher science (like metaphysics) provides the causal explanation (limmi), while the lower science (like natural science) often supplies the factual demonstration (inni) or relies on the higher for certainty. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for evaluating the strength of arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the higher and lower sciences
In the passage, the higher science (al-ʿilm al-aʿlā) is the one that provides the causal middle terms, while the lower science (al-ʿilm al-asfal) takes premises as granted. For example, natural science is lower than first philosophy (metaphysics).
2
Determine the demonstration types
The higher science generally gives a limmi (why) demonstration, establishing the cause. The lower science often relies on the higher for certainty but can provide an inni (that) demonstration, establishing the fact without the cause.
In rare cases, the higher may borrow principles from the lower, but without circularity.
3
Analyze the example of the first motion
Natural science gives the material and formal causes (nature without opposite, simple matter), while first philosophy gives the efficient and final causes (pure intellect, pure existence). Together they provide a complete limmi demonstration.
The lower alone does not give the absolute limmi.
4
Examine coordinate sciences
For sciences not subordinate (like mathematics and natural science), they may give different demonstrations for the same fact. Mathematics gives an inni demonstration (e.g., sphericity of water by evidence), while natural science gives the limmi (causal explanation).
This shows that different sciences can serve complementary roles in demonstration.

«اللّم». وأما في الأقل فربَّما أخذ العلم الأعلى مبادئ اللم من العلم الأسفل بعد أن لا تكون تلك المبادئ متوقعة في الصحة على صحة مبادٍ إنما يبين في العلم الأعلى أو يكون يبين بمباد من العلم الأعلى لكن إنما يبين بها ثانيا من العلم الأعلى مسائل ليست مبادي لها بل كما أن بعض مسائل علم واحد تكون مبادئ بالقياس إلى بعض المسائل منه بوساطة مسائل منه هي أقرب إلى المبادئ منها. فلا يبعد أن تكون مسائل علم ما يبين بمبادٍ من علم آخر ثم تصير تلك المسائل مباد بمسائل أخرى من ذلك العلم الآخر بلا دور. فيكون هذا حال مسائل تتبين في علم أسفل بمبادئ من علم أعلى ثمَّ يتبين بها مسائل ما من علم أعلى. وأما العلوم التي ليس بعضها تحتها بعض فكثيرا ما يكون أحد العلمين معطيا في مسألة واحدة بعينها برهان الإن، والآخر معطيا فيه برهان اللم كما أن العلم الرياضي يعطي في كرية الماء برهان إن بالدليل، والعلم الطبيعي يعطي برهان اللم، وكذلك في كرية الأرض وغيرها.
It gives a demonstration of why. That is because the premises in the lower science are taken as granted as subjects or postulates whose causes are unknown; and it is known that their conclusions are not certain unless certainty is obtained for their premises. Certainty for their premises is obtained only in the higher science, since the middle term is essentially in the higher science. If that demonstration is transferred to the lower science, it is in the lower science something not originally from it. As for the difference in cooperation of the two sciences regarding the why and the that, we say: As for two sciences differing in highness and non-highness, most often the demonstration giving the why from the higher to the lower is completed by the higher giving the lower premises that are the principles of the demonstration. Of this kind also is that one of them gives a demonstration whose middle term is one cause, and the other gives another demonstration whose middle term is another cause prior to that cause, which is the cause of the cause, so the lower does not give the cause completely. An example is that natural science and first philosophy share in investigating the similarity and difference of the first motion, but natural science gives the cause which is the nature that has no opposite and the simple matter that has no difference, so corruption or change cannot occur. First philosophy gives the separate efficient causality which is pure good and pure intellect, and the universal cause corresponding to the first is pure existence. The demonstrations in the two sciences are different, but natural science, though it gives some demonstration, does not give the 'demonstration of why' absolutely, but gives that it is similar as long as matter exists and that nature exists, while the higher science gives the demonstration of the permanent why absolutely, and gives the cause of the permanence of matter and the nature that has no opposite, so its effect persists. This is the most common case of the help of the higher in the 'why'. As for the less common case, sometimes the higher science takes the principles of the why from the lower science after those principles are not dependent in validity on the validity of principles that are only shown in the higher science, or they are shown by principles from the higher science but only secondarily from the higher science for issues that are not its principles, but just as some issues of a single science are principles relative to other issues of it through intermediate issues that are closer to the principles. So it is not unlikely that issues of a science are shown by principles from another science, then those issues become principles for other issues of that other science without circularity. This is the case of issues that are shown in a lower science by principles from a higher science, then by them issues from a higher science are shown. As for sciences that are not subordinate to each other, often one of the two sciences gives in one and the same issue a demonstration of that, and the other gives a demonstration of why, as the mathematical science gives in the sphericity of water a demonstration of that by evidence, and natural science gives a demonstration of why, and similarly in the sphericity of the earth and other things.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about how higher and lower sciences interact in providing demonstrations (burhan). The author begins by stating that the lower science's premises are taken as granted, and certainty is only achieved when the higher science provides the causal middle term. Then he distinguishes two cases: usually, the higher science supplies the principles for a 'why' (limmi) demonstration. An example is given of natural philosophy and first philosophy (metaphysics) investigating the first motion: natural science gives the material and formal causes, while first philosophy gives the efficient and final causes, providing the full 'why' demonstration. In a rarer case, the higher science may borrow principles from the lower science, but only after those principles are established independently. This does not lead to circularity. Finally, for coordinate sciences (not subordinate), each may give a different type of demonstration for the same thing, e.g., mathematics gives a 'that' (inni) proof of the sphericity of water, while natural science gives a 'why' proof.

Key Terms
العلم الأعلى
العلم الأسفل
البرهان اللّمّي
البرهان الإنّي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Mathematical science
B) Natural science
C) First philosophy
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The higher science provides the cause (limmi), the lower science often provides the fact (inni), and they cooperate to produce certain knowledge without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Demonstration -- Burhan

Two Types of Demonstration Across Sciences
Author explains how different sciences can provide برهان إن and برهان لم for the same issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between burhan al-inn and burhan al-limma and identify examples of each from different sciences.

Core Idea
One science proves the fact, another explains the cause.

This passage from a classical logic text explores how different sciences can address the same issue at different levels of explanation. It introduces the distinction between demonstration of the 'that' (burhan al-inn) and demonstration of the 'why' (burhan al-limma), showing that the same physical fact can be proven mathematically and explained naturally. This clarifies why different sciences are not redundant but complementary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of higher and lower sciences
The passage begins by describing how a lower science can borrow principles from a higher science without circularity. This sets the stage for the main point: sciences can cooperate in explaining the same phenomenon.
Principles (مبادي) from a higher science are used to explain issues in a lower science, and vice versa, but without circular reasoning.
2
Identify the two types of demonstration
The author introduces برهان الإن (demonstration of 'that') which establishes the existence or fact, and برهان اللم (demonstration of 'why') which provides the causal explanation. They are not mutually exclusive but complementary.
In later logic, برهان اللم is considered the perfect demonstration.
3
Analyze the example of sphericity of water
The passage says that mathematical science gives برهان الإن for the sphericity of water (by evidence, likely geometric), while natural science gives برهان اللم (explaining why water is spherical due to natural causes).
The same applies to the sphericity of the earth.
4
Apply the distinction to other Islamic science examples
Think of examples like the existence of God: in kalam, one might use a cosmological argument as برهان الإن, while a deeper metaphysical argument could be برهان اللم. Or in fiqh, the prohibition of riba can be proven by text (برهان إن) and by its harmful effects (برهان لم).
The key is that different sciences can provide different types of proof for the same claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون يبين بمباد من العلم الأعلى لكن إنما يبين بها ثانيا من العلم الأعلى مسائل ليست مبادي لها بل كما أن بعض مسائل علم واحد تكون مبادئ بالقياس إلى بعض المسائل منه بوساطة مسائل منه هي أقرب إلى المبادئ منها. فلا يبعد أن تكون مسائل علم ما يبين بمبادٍ من علم آخر ثم تصير تلك المسائل مباد بمسائل أخرى من ذلك العلم الآخر بلا دور. فيكون هذا حال مسائل تتبين في علم أسفل بمبادئ من علم أعلى ثمَّ يتبين بها مسائل ما من علم أعلى. وأما العلوم التي ليس بعضها تحتها بعض فكثيرا ما يكون أحد العلمين معطيا في مسألة واحدة بعينها برهان الإن، والآخر معطيا فيه برهان اللم كما أن العلم الرياضي يعطي في كرية الماء برهان إن بالدليل، والعلم الطبيعي يعطي برهان اللم، وكذلك في كرية الأرض وغيرها.
It is explained by principles from the higher science, but it is only explained secondarily from the higher science with issues that are not principles for it. Rather, just as some issues of a single science are principles in relation to some other issues from it through intermediary issues that are closer to the principles. So it is not improbable that issues of a science are explained by principles from another science, then those issues become principles for other issues of that other science without circularity. This is the case of issues that are explained in a lower science by principles from a higher science, then by them some issues of the higher science are explained. As for sciences, some of which are not subordinate to others, it often happens that one of the two sciences gives, for one and the same issue, the demonstration of the 'that', and the other gives the demonstration of the 'why', just as the mathematical science gives, regarding the sphericity of water, a demonstration of the 'that' by evidence, and the natural science gives the demonstration of the 'why', and similarly regarding the sphericity of the earth and other things.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between different sciences in providing demonstrations. It begins by clarifying that a lower science can use principles from a higher science without circularity. Then it introduces the distinction between demonstration of the 'that' (برهان إن) and demonstration of the 'why' (برهان لم). The key example is the sphericity of water: mathematical science proves that water is spherical (برهان إن) using geometric evidence, while natural science explains why water is spherical (برهان لم) due to natural causes. The same applies to the sphericity of the earth. This shows that the same fact can be known through different demonstrations from different sciences.

Key Takeaway
The same claim can be established by one science as a fact (برهان إن) and explained by another science as a cause (برهان لم), without circularity.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Demonstration is of Intelligible Universals, Not Sensible Particulars
Author argues that demonstration and definition are only on intelligible forms, not sensible particulars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sensible particulars and intelligible universals as objects of demonstration, using the example of the sun.

Core Idea
Demonstration and definition require the universal, not the particular.

This passage clarifies a foundational epistemological principle: demonstration and definition in the sciences deal with universal intelligible natures, not individual sensible objects. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how certain knowledge is attained in Islamic sciences like Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence where the author states directly that demonstration is about the intelligible, not the sensible. Note the contrast.
The phrase 'البرهان على المعقول دون المحسوس' is the key.
2
Analyze the example of the sun
Read how the author applies the claim to the sun: demonstration does not apply to 'this sun' as a particular, but to the universal nature of 'sun' abstracted from accidents.
Notice the phrase 'لا من جهة ما هي هذه الشمس بل من جهة أنها شمس'.
3
Understand the reason: abstraction from matter
Explain why this is necessary: sensible particulars are perishable and changeable, while demonstration requires stable, necessary truths. Only intelligible forms are abstracted from matter and thus incorruptible.
4
Apply to other sensible objects
Generalize the principle: for any sensible object, demonstration and definition are only possible when considering its universal nature, not its individuality.
For example, 'water' in fiqh rulings is considered as a universal liquid, not a particular cup.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من البين أنه لا سبيل إلى إقامة البرهان في العلوم على مبادئها وإلا فما يبين به المبدأ هو المبدأ؛ والعلم به أحق من العلم بما قيل إنه مبدأ له. والمبادئ للعلوم قد تكون بينة بنفسها، وقد لا تكون بل تكون محتاجة إلى بيان؛ وإنما يكون بيانها في علم آخر. وكذلك الأصول الموضوعة فإن منها ما يكون بينا بنفسه، ومنها ما لا يكون كذلك بل يكون مفتقرًا إلى البيان. وأما البرهان فإنه على المعقول دون المحسوس؛ والموجودات كلها إما محسوسة وإما معقولة. والمحسوسة ليست مبرهنا عليها، ولا محدودًا من جهة ما هي محسوسة وشخصية بل من جهة طبيعة عقلية. فالبرهان لا يقوم على الشمس من جهة ما هي هذه الشمس بل من جهة أنها شمس مجردة عن سائر العوارض. وكذلك الحد ليس لها من جهة ما هي هذه الشمس فإذا كان كذلك كان البرهان على صورة معقولة مجردة عن المادة؛ لئلا تكون محسوسة ولا قابلة للفساد فكذلك الحد.
Then it is clear that there is no way to establish demonstration in the sciences on their own principles; otherwise, what is used to explain the principle is the principle, and knowledge of it has more right than knowledge of what is said to be a principle for it. The principles of the sciences may be self-evident, or they may not be, but rather need elucidation; and their elucidation is only in another science. Likewise, the posited axioms, some of them are self-evident, and some are not, but are in need of elucidation. As for demonstration, it is about the intelligible, not the sensible; and all existents are either sensible or intelligible. The sensible are not demonstrated, nor defined, insofar as they are sensible and individual, but rather from the aspect of an intelligible nature. For demonstration does not hold of the sun insofar as it is this sun, but insofar as it is a sun abstracted from all other accidents. Likewise, the definition is not for it insofar as it is this sun. Therefore, demonstration is on an intelligible form abstracted from matter, so that it is neither sensible nor subject to corruption; likewise the definition.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage clarifies the subject matter of demonstration and definition. It first notes that demonstrations cannot be based on the principles of the same science without circularity; principles may be self-evident or require another science. Then it states that demonstration concerns the intelligible (معقول), not the sensible (محسوس). Sensible particulars are not demonstrable or definable as such; they are only considered under a universal intelligible nature. The example of the sun is used: demonstration does not apply to 'this sun' as a particular, but to 'sun' as a universal nature abstracted from accidents. Similarly, definition is of the universal. Therefore, demonstration and definition are of intelligible forms abstracted from matter, making them incorruptible and stable.

Demonstration -- Burhan

How Matter Enters Definitions: Mathematical vs Natural Forms
Author distinguishes mathematical from natural forms and discusses demonstration on intelligibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mathematical and natural forms based on whether matter enters their definition, and explain the epistemological implications.

Core Idea
Natural forms cannot be defined without their matter; mathematical forms are defined abstractly.

In this passage, the author classifies existing things based on the relationship between their form and matter. Understanding this classification is crucial for grasping the scope of definition and demonstration. Let's see how the author distinguishes mathematical from natural forms and what this means for definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of forms
Look for the categories mentioned: forms absolutely separate from matter (divine), forms abstract in definition but not existence (mathematical), and forms tied to specific matter (natural). Note which ones have matter entering their definition.
The passage mentions 'separate forms' (suwar mufāraqa) and 'abstract in definition' (intizāʿiyya bi-l-ḥadd).
2
Examine the definition of mathematical objects
The passage says mathematical objects are abstract in definition; their subjects are not determined by species. This means their definitions do not include matter, even though they exist in matter.
For example, a triangle is defined by its properties, not as a particular bronze triangle.
3
Examine natural forms
Each natural form has a matter suitable to it by species; matter enters its definition because the form's nature is specific to that matter. Without that matter, the form would not be what it is.
A horse, for instance, is defined as a certain animal with flesh and bones—matter is part of its definition.
4
Relate to demonstration
Since demonstration requires abstraction from matter, natural forms are not directly demonstrable; they require abstraction of their universal nature. This sets the stage for understanding the object of demonstration.
This connects to the next lesson on demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجودات التي صورها مفارقة المواد على الإطلاق. ومنها ما هي انتزاعية بالحد كالعلوم الرياضية فإن موضوعاتها أمور غير مفارقة بالذات للموضوعات ولكن مفارقته للحدود بها. وذلك لأن موضوعاتها أمور غير معينة بالنوع. وأما الصور الطبيعية فإن لكل واحد منها مادة ملائمة لها بالنوع لا يمكن أن تكون مفارقة لها، ولا في مادة أخرى، فطباع تلك الصورة مختصة بتلك المادة فلذلك تدخل المواد في حدودها. والأمور الطبيعية هي التي تجتمع منها بالذات هذه العلل كلها؛ والكلام في العلل وكونها حدودًا وسطى في البراهين تعرف من بعد على سبيل التفصيل إن شاء الله تعالى. ثم من البين أنه لا سبيل إلى إقامة البرهان في العلوم على مبادئها وإلا فما يبين به المبدأ هو المبدأ؛ والعلم به أحق من العلم بما قيل إنه مبدأ له. والمبادئ للعلوم قد تكون بينة بنفسها، وقد لا تكون بل تكون محتاجة إلى بيان؛ وإنما يكون بيانها في علم آخر. وكذلك الأصول الموضوعة فإن منها ما يكون بينا بنفسه، ومنها ما لا يكون كذلك بل يكون مفتقرًا إلى البيان. وأما البرهان فإنه على المعقول دون المحسوس؛ والموجودات كلها إما محسوسة وإما معقولة. والمحسوسة ليست مبرهنا عليها، ولا محدودًا من جهة ما هي محسوسة وشخصية بل من جهة طبيعة عقلية. فالبرهان لا يقوم على الشمس من جهة ما هي هذه الشمس بل من جهة أنها شمس مجردة عن سائر العوارض. وكذلك الحد ليس لها من جهة ما هي هذه الشمس فإذا كان كذلك كان البرهان على صورة معقولة مجردة عن المادة؛ لئلا تكون محسوسة ولا قابلة للفساد فكذلك الحد. واعلم بأن أفلاطون جعل الصور المفارقة موجودة لكل معقول حتى الطبيعيات فسماها مثلاً إذا كانت مجردة عن المادة، وإذا اقتربت بالمادة صورًا طبيعية؛ وفيه نظر فإن الصور الطبيعية لا تكون هي هي إذا جردت عن المادة، والصور التعليمية لا تقوم بلا مادة وإن كانت بالحد لا بالمادة؛ والكلام في مثل هذا إنما هو في الفلسفة الأولى دون المنطق بل حسن في المنطق أن يبين أن هذه وإن فرضت موجودة فلا مدخل لها في علم البرهان ولا هي موضوعة لهذه البراهين التي نحن في تعليمها. فإن هذه البراهين وإن كانت بالذات وأولا لأمور عقلية كلية فإنها ثانيًا، وبالعرض لأمور محسوسة؛ إذ كل حكم يصح على الشمس المطلقة يصح على هذه الشمس؛ وعلى هذا في سائر الأشياء.
Existing things whose forms are separate from matter absolutely. And among them are those that are abstract in definition like the mathematical sciences, for their subjects are things not separate in essence from subjects but separate from them in definition. That is because their subjects are things not determined by species. As for natural forms, each one has a matter suitable to it by species that cannot be separate from it, nor in another matter. So the nature of that form is specific to that matter; therefore matters enter into their definitions. And natural things are those in which all these causes come together essentially; and the discussion of causes and their being middle terms in demonstrations will be known later in detail, God willing. Then it is clear that there is no way to establish demonstration in the sciences on their principles; otherwise what is used to demonstrate the principle would be the principle; and knowledge of it is more deserving than knowledge of what is said to be a principle for it. And the principles of the sciences may be self-evident, or they may not be but rather need to be made evident; and their demonstration is in another science. Similarly, the posited axioms: some are self-evident and some are not but rather need demonstration. As for demonstration, it is about the intelligible, not the sensible. And all existing things are either sensible or intelligible. Sensible things are not demonstrated, nor defined, insofar as they are sensible and individual, but rather from an intellectual nature. So demonstration does not apply to the sun insofar as it is this sun, but insofar as it is a sun abstracted from all accidents. Likewise, definition does not apply to it insofar as it is this sun. So if that is the case, demonstration is about an intelligible form abstracted from matter, so that it is not sensible nor subject to corruption. Similarly for definition. And know that Plato made the separate forms exist for every intelligible, even natural things, calling them exemplars when abstracted from matter, and when connected to matter, natural forms. But there is objection: natural forms are not the same when abstracted from matter, and mathematical forms do not subsist without matter, even if definitionally not materially. Discussion of such matters belongs to first philosophy, not logic. Rather, it is good in logic to show that even if these are assumed to exist, they have no place in the science of demonstration, nor are they subjects of these demonstrations we are teaching. For these demonstrations, even if essentially and primarily they are about universal intelligible things, are secondarily and accidentally about sensible things, since every judgement that applies to the absolute sun applies to this sun; and likewise for all things.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of the nature of demonstration (burhan) and its objects. The author begins by distinguishing types of existing things: some have forms absolutely separate from matter (presumably divine or intellective), while others are abstract in definition but not in existence, like mathematical objects. Natural forms, however, are tied to specific matter and cannot be abstracted; matter enters their definitions. The author then turns to demonstration, noting that it cannot be used to prove the principles of a science itself, as that would lead to circularity. Principles may be self-evident or proven in another science. Crucially, demonstration applies to intelligible, not sensible, things. A sensible particular like 'this sun' is not demonstrated or defined qua particular, but only via its universal intellectual nature. Thus demonstration and definition deal with forms abstracted from matter, avoiding sensibleness and corruption. The author critiques Plato's theory of separate forms, arguing that natural forms lose identity when abstracted, and mathematical forms require matter. Such metaphysical debates belong to first philosophy, not logic. However, it is useful to note that even if Platonic forms existed, they play no role in logical demonstration. Finally, the author clarifies that demonstrations primarily concern universal intelligibles, but secondarily they apply to sensible particulars because any judgement true of the universal is also true of the particular—for example, a judgement about 'sun' in the abstract applies to this specific sun.

Key Terms
صور مفارقة
انتزاعية بالحد
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because natural forms are separate from matter
B) Because each natural form has a specific matter suitable to it
C) Because Plato said so
D) Because definitions must include causes

Key Takeaway
Matter is integral to the definition of natural forms, distinguishing them from mathematical forms which are defined abstractly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Demonstration -- Burhan

Demonstration Applies to Intelligibles, Not Sensibles
Author distinguishes mathematical from natural forms and discusses demonstration on intelligibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why demonstration cannot be directly applied to sensible particulars and how it nevertheless relates to them secondarily.

Core Idea
Demonstration deals with universal intelligible natures, not individual sensible objects.

Having understood the distinction between forms, we now explore the nature of demonstration itself. The author argues that demonstration and definition are about intelligible forms abstracted from matter. Sensible particulars are only covered secondarily, through their universal nature. This is a cornerstone of the epistemology of demonstration in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the claim about demonstration
Find the sentence: 'As for demonstration, it is about the intelligible, not the sensible.' This is the central thesis. Underline it.
The Arabic: 'وأما البرهان فإنه على المعقول دون المحسوس'
2
Understand the example of the sun
The author uses the sun as an example. Demonstration does not apply to 'this sun' (a particular sensible object) but to 'sun' in the abstract—the universal nature. Why? Because sensible particulars are subject to corruption and individuation, whereas demonstration requires universality and necessity.
Think of the difference between 'this cup of water' and 'water' as a substance.
3
Recognize the primary vs. secondary application
The author explains that demonstrations are primarily about universal intelligibles, but secondarily they apply to sensible particulars. This is because a judgement about the universal holds for each particular instance. So while we cannot demonstrate 'this sun' directly, the demonstration of 'sun' applies to it.
This avoids the problem of particulars being unknowable by demonstration.
4
Note the implication for definitions
Similarly, definition does not define a particular sensible but the universal nature. Defining 'this sun' is impossible; we define 'sun' as a species.
This parallels the earlier point that definition deals with forms abstracted from matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجودات التي صورها مفارقة المواد على الإطلاق. ومنها ما هي انتزاعية بالحد كالعلوم الرياضية فإن موضوعاتها أمور غير مفارقة بالذات للموضوعات ولكن مفارقته للحدود بها. وذلك لأن موضوعاتها أمور غير معينة بالنوع. وأما الصور الطبيعية فإن لكل واحد منها مادة ملائمة لها بالنوع لا يمكن أن تكون مفارقة لها، ولا في مادة أخرى، فطباع تلك الصورة مختصة بتلك المادة فلذلك تدخل المواد في حدودها. والأمور الطبيعية هي التي تجتمع منها بالذات هذه العلل كلها؛ والكلام في العلل وكونها حدودًا وسطى في البراهين تعرف من بعد على سبيل التفصيل إن شاء الله تعالى. ثم من البين أنه لا سبيل إلى إقامة البرهان في العلوم على مبادئها وإلا فما يبين به المبدأ هو المبدأ؛ والعلم به أحق من العلم بما قيل إنه مبدأ له. والمبادئ للعلوم قد تكون بينة بنفسها، وقد لا تكون بل تكون محتاجة إلى بيان؛ وإنما يكون بيانها في علم آخر. وكذلك الأصول الموضوعة فإن منها ما يكون بينا بنفسه، ومنها ما لا يكون كذلك بل يكون مفتقرًا إلى البيان. وأما البرهان فإنه على المعقول دون المحسوس؛ والموجودات كلها إما محسوسة وإما معقولة. والمحسوسة ليست مبرهنا عليها، ولا محدودًا من جهة ما هي محسوسة وشخصية بل من جهة طبيعة عقلية. فالبرهان لا يقوم على الشمس من جهة ما هي هذه الشمس بل من جهة أنها شمس مجردة عن سائر العوارض. وكذلك الحد ليس لها من جهة ما هي هذه الشمس فإذا كان كذلك كان البرهان على صورة معقولة مجردة عن المادة؛ لئلا تكون محسوسة ولا قابلة للفساد فكذلك الحد. واعلم بأن أفلاطون جعل الصور المفارقة موجودة لكل معقول حتى الطبيعيات فسماها مثلاً إذا كانت مجردة عن المادة، وإذا اقتربت بالمادة صورًا طبيعية؛ وفيه نظر فإن الصور الطبيعية لا تكون هي هي إذا جردت عن المادة، والصور التعليمية لا تقوم بلا مادة وإن كانت بالحد لا بالمادة؛ والكلام في مثل هذا إنما هو في الفلسفة الأولى دون المنطق بل حسن في المنطق أن يبين أن هذه وإن فرضت موجودة فلا مدخل لها في علم البرهان ولا هي موضوعة لهذه البراهين التي نحن في تعليمها. فإن هذه البراهين وإن كانت بالذات وأولا لأمور عقلية كلية فإنها ثانيًا، وبالعرض لأمور محسوسة؛ إذ كل حكم يصح على الشمس المطلقة يصح على هذه الشمس؛ وعلى هذا في سائر الأشياء.
Existing things whose forms are separate from matter absolutely. And among them are those that are abstract in definition like the mathematical sciences, for their subjects are things not separate in essence from subjects but separate from them in definition. That is because their subjects are things not determined by species. As for natural forms, each one has a matter suitable to it by species that cannot be separate from it, nor in another matter. So the nature of that form is specific to that matter; therefore matters enter into their definitions. And natural things are those in which all these causes come together essentially; and the discussion of causes and their being middle terms in demonstrations will be known later in detail, God willing. Then it is clear that there is no way to establish demonstration in the sciences on their principles; otherwise what is used to demonstrate the principle would be the principle; and knowledge of it is more deserving than knowledge of what is said to be a principle for it. And the principles of the sciences may be self-evident, or they may not be but rather need to be made evident; and their demonstration is in another science. Similarly, the posited axioms: some are self-evident and some are not but rather need demonstration. As for demonstration, it is about the intelligible, not the sensible. And all existing things are either sensible or intelligible. Sensible things are not demonstrated, nor defined, insofar as they are sensible and individual, but rather from an intellectual nature. So demonstration does not apply to the sun insofar as it is this sun, but insofar as it is a sun abstracted from all accidents. Likewise, definition does not apply to it insofar as it is this sun. So if that is the case, demonstration is about an intelligible form abstracted from matter, so that it is not sensible nor subject to corruption. Similarly for definition. And know that Plato made the separate forms exist for every intelligible, even natural things, calling them exemplars when abstracted from matter, and when connected to matter, natural forms. But there is objection: natural forms are not the same when abstracted from matter, and mathematical forms do not subsist without matter, even if definitionally not materially. Discussion of such matters belongs to first philosophy, not logic. Rather, it is good in logic to show that even if these are assumed to exist, they have no place in the science of demonstration, nor are they subjects of these demonstrations we are teaching. For these demonstrations, even if essentially and primarily they are about universal intelligible things, are secondarily and accidentally about sensible things, since every judgement that applies to the absolute sun applies to this sun; and likewise for all things.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of the nature of demonstration (burhan) and its objects. The author begins by distinguishing types of existing things: some have forms absolutely separate from matter (presumably divine or intellective), while others are abstract in definition but not in existence, like mathematical objects. Natural forms, however, are tied to specific matter and cannot be abstracted; matter enters their definitions. The author then turns to demonstration, noting that it cannot be used to prove the principles of a science itself, as that would lead to circularity. Principles may be self-evident or proven in another science. Crucially, demonstration applies to intelligible, not sensible, things. A sensible particular like 'this sun' is not demonstrated or defined qua particular, but only via its universal intellectual nature. Thus demonstration and definition deal with forms abstracted from matter, avoiding sensibleness and corruption. The author critiques Plato's theory of separate forms, arguing that natural forms lose identity when abstracted, and mathematical forms require matter. Such metaphysical debates belong to first philosophy, not logic. However, it is useful to note that even if Platonic forms existed, they play no role in logical demonstration. Finally, the author clarifies that demonstrations primarily concern universal intelligibles, but secondarily they apply to sensible particulars because any judgement true of the universal is also true of the particular—for example, a judgement about 'sun' in the abstract applies to this specific sun.

انتزاعية بالحد

Demonstration is fundamentally about universal intelligible forms, but valid judgements about universals apply to sensible particulars secondarily.

Demonstration -- Burhan
intermediate

Dialectic vs. Demonstration: Scope in Principles, Subjects, and Problems
Contrast of dialectic and demonstration in scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast dialectic and demonstration in terms of their scope regarding principles, subjects, and problems, using the distinctions from this passage.

Core Idea
Dialectic is unlimited in all three aspects; demonstration is strictly limited.

In this passage, the author systematically compares dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan) across three dimensions: principles, subjects, and problems. Understanding this contrast is crucial for knowing when to use which method in Islamic sciences. Demonstration is the gold standard for certainty, but dialectic plays a vital role in exploring opposing views and in educational contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aspects compared
Find where the author mentions subjects (mawḍū‘āt), problems (masā’il), and principles (mabādi’). These are the three dimensions used to contrast dialectic and demonstration.
2
Note dialectic's scope in subjects
The author says dialectic does not restrict itself to one subject; all subjects are equal to it. Demonstration, by contrast, restricts itself to one subject.
3
Understand dialectic's unlimitedness in problems
The author gives two reasons: (1) dialectic considers both intrinsic and foreign problems (e.g., geometrical questions beyond the subject), and (2) it may support both contradictory sides at different times using two syllogisms.
4
Understand dialectic's unlimitedness in principles
The author gives two reasons: (1) dialectic does not restrict itself to principles intrinsic to the thing, and (2) it can take primary principles and common opinions (even if not true) and use principles from the opponent for each side.
5
Contrast with demonstration
Demonstration is limited in all three: it has a specific subject, deals only with problems intrinsic to that subject, and uses only truthful, intrinsic principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

علم في المسائل كما يشارك في المبادئ الواجب قبولها. وكما يشارك في الموضوعات فإنه لا يختص بموضوع لكن الجدل ليس بمحدود النظر في شيء من الوجوه؛ وكل علم فإنه محدود النظر في شيء من الوجوه الثلاثة أعني الموضوعات والمسائل والمبادئ وأما أن الجدل ليس بمحدود النظر في الموضوعات؛ فلأنه لا يقتصر على موضوع واحد يبحث عن أحواله بل الجميع عنده سواء؛ والبرهان يقتصر عليه. وأما أنه ليس بمحدود النظر في المسائل فذلك من وجهين: أحدهما أنه لا يقتصر على المسائل الذاتية بالموضوع في الوقت بل وفي الغريبة أيضًا مثل أنه ليس بنظر هل الخط المستقيم إذا قام عليه خط كان كذا وكذا بل هو أحسن من المستدير أو ليس؟ وهل هو مضاد المستدير أو ليس ؟ والثاني لأنه قد يتفق أن ينصر الضدين في النقيضين معا بقياسين في وقتين كل واحد منهما جدلي على ما ستعرفه. وأما أنه ليس بمحدود النظر في المبادئ فذلك من جهتين : إحديهما أنه لا ما في على ما عرف. وأما البرهان فإنه محدود الموضوع محدود المسألة محدود المبادئ. والمسألة هي التي يبينها بالبرهان وينصرها ويكاد أن يكون الحق هو أنه ليس في العلوم مسألة عن طريقي النقيض ولكن قد يقال مسألة علمية على وجهين: أحدهما أنه ينفع في التعليم والتعلم وهو أحد طرفي النقيض على التعيين أنه هو الحق وأنه لا يتعداه المجيب والسائل؛ وإنما قيل للتقرير. والثاني في المخاطبات الامتحانية التي تكون في العلوم، والمسائل الامتحانية علمية من جهة أن مباديها مناسبة وليست علمية من جهة أن الغرض فيها ليس إثبات علم . فلذلك إذا حققت لم تكن علمية برهانية مطلقة بل المسائل العلمية المطلقة محدودة؛ وليس كل سؤال هندسيّاً ولا حسابيًا ولا طبيا مثلا بل المسألة الهندسية إما عن مقدمة بانت بالطرق الهندسية ويراد أن بيانها غيرها فيكون عن مبدأ خاص بالمطلوب. وإما عن مبدأ عام للمسائل الهندسية تبين بها المطالب الهندسية ولا يتبين The principles that must be accepted are shared among the sciences, not as that which has the demonstration, i.e., subjects, or that for which the demonstration exists, i.e., problems, but rather as that which is in the demonstrator. Dialectic uses them in the sense that they are mashhurat (common opinions), so it also shares with every science in problems as it shares in the principles that must be accepted. And just as it shares in subjects, for it is not restricted to a subject, but dialectic is not limited in view in any of the three aspects, meaning subjects, problems, and principles. As for dialectic not being limited in view in subjects, it is because it does not restrict itself to one subject to investigate its states, but all are equal to it; demonstration restricts itself to one subject. As for it not being limited in view in problems, that is from two aspects: first, it does not restrict itself to problems intrinsic to the subject in time, but even to foreign ones, such as whether a straight line, when a line stands on it, is such-and-such, or is it better than the curved or not? And whether it is opposite to the curved or not? Second, because it may happen that it supports both opposites in the two contradictories together with two syllogisms at two times, each of which is dialectical, as you will know. As for it not being limited in view in principles, that is from two aspects: first, it does not restrict itself to principles intrinsic to the thing, but whatever happens; second, it takes primary principles and true common opinions that are not true together, and what it accepts from the respondent, and it may make for each of the two contradictory views a principle for its syllogism; that at one time, this at another time, as has been known. As for demonstration, it is limited in subject, limited in problem, limited in principles. The problem is that which is demonstrated and upheld by demonstration, and it is almost true that there is no problem in the sciences about both sides of the contradiction, but it may be called a scientific problem in two ways: first, it is useful in teaching and learning, and it is one of the two sides of the contradiction determined as the truth, and the responder and questioner do not go beyond it; it is said for confirmation. Second, in examination discussions that occur in the sciences, and examination problems are scientific in the sense that their principles are appropriate, but not scientific in the sense that the purpose is not to establish scientific knowledge. Therefore, when investigated, they are not absolutely scientific demonstrative problems, but absolutely scientific problems are limited. Not every question is geometrical or arithmetical or medical, for example, but the geometrical problem is either about a premise that has become clear by geometrical methods and its clarification is intended by something else, so it is about a principle specific to the required conclusion, or about a general principle for geometric problems by which geometric objectives are clarified, but it itself is not clarified in geometry. Similarly, theoretical problems are either special theoretical problems clarified in that science, or they are geometrical—
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage compares dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan) in terms of their scope regarding principles, subjects, and problems. The author states that dialectic uses principles that are common to sciences, but not as the subjects or problems of demonstration; rather, dialectic uses them as mashhurat (common opinions). Dialectic is not limited in subject because it does not restrict itself to a single subject; demonstration does. Dialectic is not limited in problems for two reasons: it considers both intrinsic and foreign problems, and it may support both contradictory sides at different times. Dialectic is not limited in principles for two reasons: it does not restrict to intrinsic principles, and it takes primary principles and common opinions even if not true, and it may adopt principles for each side. Demonstration, in contrast, is limited in all three. The passage then discusses the nature of scientific problems, distinguishing between problems for teaching/learning (which are determined as one side of the contradiction) and examination problems (which may not aim at establishing scientific knowledge). It concludes by noting that not every question is within the scope of a specific science; problems may be about particular premises or general principles of that science.

Demonstration -- Burhan
