Knowledge of That vs. Knowledge of Why
Author classifies types of knowledge with natural science examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish examples of 'ilm bi-inn and 'ilm bi-lim from Islamic scientific texts.

Core Idea
Knowing the cause is not the same as knowing the fact.

In Islamic logic, there is a fundamental distinction between knowing that something is the case and knowing why it is the case. This lesson unpacks that distinction using classical examples from astronomy, optics, and medicine.

Guided Path
1
Define the two terms
Locate in the passage the definitions of 'ilm bi-inn and 'ilm bi-lim. 'Ilm bi-inn is knowledge of a fact without the cause; 'ilm bi-lim is knowledge of the cause.
The term 'ilm bi-lim is often associated with burhan (demonstration) that provides the cause.
2
Apply to the sailor-astronomer example
The author says the sailor has 'that' about weather, while the astronomer has 'why' about celestial motions. This shows that practical knowledge often lacks causal depth.
The sailor senses the phenomenon directly, while the astronomer possesses the theoretical cause.
3
Test with the wound healing example
The physician knows that round wounds heal slowly; the geometer knows why because the circle is the most encompassing shape. This illustrates cross-science transfer of 'why'.
The 'that' belongs to medicine, the 'why' to geometry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العلم بإنَّ لمن يحس بالأمر والعلم بلم فذلك لأصحاب التعاليم: يعني أن العلم بإن هو للملاح، والعلم بلم هو للمنجم. وعلى هذا وقيل لأن أصحاب العلوم العالية عندهم السبب ؛ وكثيرًا ما لا يحسون بالجزئيات ولا يشعرون لها على ما هي عليها؛ وهذا هو الأكثر بالنسبة. وقد يكون على وجه بإن وهو أن يكون جزء من علم تحت علم آخر لا كله مثل إن النظر في الهالة والقوس وما أشبه ذلك من الجمالات الكلية من انعكاس البصر إلى [؟] أو ملوّن أملس صقيل جزء من العلم الطبيعي وموضوع تحت علم المناظر : ثم تحت علم الهندسة والعلم كله ليس كذلك فيكون عند الطبيعي أن القوس هي هكذا وهكذا بسبب كذا غير محصل، وعند المناظري أنه لم هو بالسبب المحصل المقرر. وقد يكون على وجه ثالث وهو أنه قد يتفق أن لا يكون العلم كله ولا جزء ما معين تحت علم آخر بل مسألة ما بعينها؛ إذ يتفق بعرض عارض غريب لموضوع الصناعة مثل استدارة الجرح. فإن هذا العارض موجب عارضًا ذاتيا وهو عسير الانزمال فيكون باقتران عارض غريب منهما لالتزام عارض ذاتي فيكون برهانه المعطي اللم لا من ذلك العلم بل من العلم الذي منه العارض الغريب. فالطبيب يحكم أن الجراحات المستديرة بطيئة الانزمال، والمهندس يعطي العلة في ذلك حين يقول إن الدائرة أوسع الأشكال إحاطة. وقد يمكن أن يكون السبب مركباً من العلم الطبيعي والهندسي فيقال لأن الانزمال إنما يتم بحركة إلى الوسط : فإذا كانت زاوية تعينت جهة الحركة فيسهل الالتقاء. وإذا لم يكن زاوية كانت الحركة في جميع المحيط معا؛ وتعاونت الأجزاء وأبطأ الانزمال.
Then knowledge of 'that' belongs to one who senses the matter, and knowledge of 'why' belongs to the practitioners of the sciences; meaning that knowledge of 'that' is for the sailor, and knowledge of 'why' is for the astronomer. And upon this it is said that the practitioners of the higher sciences possess the cause, and often they do not sense the particulars nor perceive them as they are; and this is the case for the most part. And it may be in a way of 'that', which is that a part of a science falls under another science, not the whole, such as the study of the halo, the rainbow, and similar general phenomena, from the reflection of sight to a [؟] or a smooth, polished colored object, is a part of natural science and falls under the science of optics, then under the science of geometry; but the whole science is not like that. Thus, the natural scientist has that the rainbow is such and such due to such a cause without specification, and the optical scientist has why it is so with the precise specified cause. And it may be in a third way, which is that it may happen that neither the whole science nor a specific part is under another science, but rather a particular question, as it occurs due to an accidental attribute of the subject of the craft, such as the roundness of a wound. For this attribute necessitates a self-attribute, and it is difficult to heal; thus, through the conjunction of a foreign accident from them for the entailment of a self-accident, its demonstration giving the 'why' comes not from that science but from the science from which the foreign accident originates. So the physician judges that round wounds heal slowly, and the geometer gives the cause for that when he says that the circle is the most encompassing of shapes. And it is possible that the cause is composed of natural and geometric science, so it is said that healing occurs only by movement toward the middle; so when there is an angle, the direction of movement is determined and convergence becomes easy. When there is no angle, movement is along the entire circumference simultaneously, the parts cooperate, and healing is slow.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a classification and elaboration of two types of knowledge: knowledge of 'that' (al-'ilm bi-inn) and knowledge of 'why' (al-'ilm bi-lim). The author begins by assigning 'that' to those who sense the matter (e.g., the sailor who knows the weather but not the cause) and 'why' to practitioners of higher sciences (e.g., the astronomer who knows the cause of celestial phenomena). The general principle is that practitioners of higher sciences possess the cause but often lack detailed perception of particulars. 

The author then presents three scenarios for how these types of knowledge relate across sciences:

1. A part of one science falls under another science entirely. Example: the study of halo, rainbow, etc., is part of natural science but falls under optics and then geometry. The natural scientist knows 'that' the phenomenon occurs with a vague cause, while the optical scientist knows 'why' with precise cause.

2. Neither the whole science nor a specific part falls under another, but a particular question arises due to an accidental attribute. Example: the roundness of a wound—a medical phenomenon—requires a geometric explanation. The physician knows 'that' round wounds heal slowly, and the geometer supplies the cause ('why') because the circle has the greatest area relative to its boundary, delaying healing.

3. The cause may be compound, involving both natural and geometric sciences: healing involves movement toward the center; when there is an angle (e.g., an angular wound), movement is directed and healing is easier; when no angle (circular), movement is uniform around the circumference, delaying healing.

Throughout, the author distinguishes the practical knowledge of particulars from the theoretical knowledge of causes, emphasizing that 'why' often belongs to a different science than 'that'.

Key Terms
العلم بإن
العلم بلم
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, who provides the 'why' for the slow healing of round wounds?
A) The physician, because he treats the wound.
B) The geometer, because he explains the geometric property of circles.
C) The natural scientist, because the wound is a natural object.
D) The optical scientist, because it involves light.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of fact and knowledge of cause are distinct; often the cause lies in a different science from the fact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the following statement by Ibn Sina: 'The eclipse occurs because the moon passes between the earth and the sun.' Identify whether this is 'ilm bi-inn or 'ilm bi-lim and justify from the passage.
?
From the hadith sciences, a narrator reports that the Prophet ﷺ forbade selling dates for dates (that it is riba). The jurist explains that the prohibition is due to the 'illah of weight. Classify each as 'that' or 'why'.

Borrowing Explanations Across Sciences
Author illustrates three ways 'that' and 'why' relate across sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a multi-scientific explanation and determine which science provides the 'that' and which provides the 'why'.

Core Idea
A single phenomenon often requires causes from multiple sciences.

The passage describes three scenarios where the 'why' of a phenomenon comes from a science different from the one that observes the 'that'. This lesson helps you map these cross-scientific dependencies using Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
List the three scenarios
The author presents: (1) a part of one science falls under another; (2) a particular question arises from an accidental attribute; (3) the cause is compound from two sciences.
Scenario 1: optics under geometry. Scenario 2: wound roundness. Scenario 3: healing involving natural and geometric causes.
2
Analyze the rainbow scenario
The rainbow is a natural phenomenon, but its precise explanation ('why') comes from optics and geometry. The natural scientist gives a vague 'that'.
Note the hierarchy: natural science -> optics -> geometry.
3
Examine the wound scenario
The physician's 'that' from medicine, the geometer's 'why' from geometry. This is scenario 2 where an accidental attribute (roundness) forces a cross-science explanation.
The wound is a medical subject, but the cause is geometric.
4
Understand compound causes
Healing involves movement toward the center: a geometric principle combined with a natural one. This shows that 'why' may be composite.
The analogy: angular wounds heal faster because movement is directed; circular wounds have uniform movement, slowing healing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why Syllogism Cannot Produce Ignorance
Author refutes the idea that syllogism can produce ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential from accidental causation in syllogism and explain why simple ignorance cannot be produced by syllogism according to this passage.

Core Idea
A syllogism only removes falsehood; it never creates ignorance.

Some might think that if a syllogism refutes a false opinion without clarifying the truth, it leaves the mind in ignorance. The author corrects this: syllogisms work essentially to invalidate falsehood, and any resulting ignorance is accidental. Understanding this protects the epistemological role of syllogism in Islamic sciences, where proofs are tools for discovery, not for generating doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ignorance
The passage distinguishes between simple ignorance (absence of knowledge) and other types (e.g., false belief). Locate where the author defines simple ignorance as 'the absence of knowledge in the soul only'.
2
Analyze why simple ignorance cannot be acquired by syllogism
The author argues that simple ignorance is merely a negation of knowledge. A syllogism, being a form of reasoning, cannot produce a negation directly; it only operates on propositions. So, the idea that syllogism 'causes' ignorance is false in itself.
3
Examine the argument against equivalence of proofs (takafu' al-hujaj)
Some claim that when proofs are equally strong on both sides, they produce doubt and thus ignorance. The author counters: equivalence only preserves existing ignorance; it does not create it. Distinguish between 'preserving' and 'producing'.
4
Distinguish essential vs. accidental causation
The author states that a syllogism essentially (bi al-dhat) establishes the invalidity of a false opinion. If the truth is not clarified, any resulting ignorance is accidental (bi al-'arad). This means the syllogism's primary effect is to negate falsehood, not to leave a void.
This distinction is crucial for evaluating the effects of arguments in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد علمت أن الجهل ليس صيغا واحدا بل من الجهل ما هو بسيط وهو عدم العلم في النفس فقط؛ وإنه لا يكتسب بقياس فإنه سلب العلم فقط، وخلو النفس عنه وإن كان قد يظن أنه يكتسب بقياس . وذلك أن تكافؤ الحجج يوجب هذا الجهل ؛ وهذا خطأ؛ إذ التكافؤ يثبت هذا الجهل الموجود ويحفظه. وأما أن يحدثه فليس وأما على ما ظنه بعضهم أن الرأي الباطل إذا فسد بحجج ولم يتّضح بفساده الحق يوجب ذلك الجهل ؛ وكان بقياس، وإنه خطأ أيضًا؛ إذ القياس لا يحدثه لذاته بل يحدث بالعرض؛ إذ القياس إنما يوجب بالذات بطلان الرأي الفاسد فلا يبطل ذلك. ولم يكن هناك رأي آخر عرض خلو
And you have known that ignorance is not one type, but of ignorance there is that which is simple, which is the absence of knowledge in the soul only; and it is not acquired through syllogism, for it is merely the negation of knowledge, and the soul's emptiness of it, even though it might be thought that it is acquired through syllogism. That is because the equivalence of proofs necessitates this ignorance; but this is wrong; for equivalence preserves and maintains this existing ignorance. As for producing it, it does not. And as for what some have thought that a false opinion, when it is refuted by proofs and the truth is not clarified by its corruption, necessitates that ignorance, and it was by syllogism, that is also wrong; for the syllogism does not produce it in itself, but produces it accidentally; for the syllogism only necessitates in itself the invalidity of the false opinion, so it does not negate that. And there was no other opinion that occurred, emptiness.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage deals with the nature of ignorance and its relation to syllogism. The author distinguishes between simple ignorance (absence of knowledge) and other types. He argues that simple ignorance cannot be produced by a syllogism because a syllogism only removes false opinions; it does not create ignorance. If someone claims that equivocal arguments (takaful al-hujaj) cause ignorance, the author responds that such arguments only preserve existing ignorance, not produce it. Similarly, if a false opinion is refuted but the truth is not clarified, that does not generate ignorance; rather, the syllogism's essential role is to invalidate falsehood. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing with a distinction between essential and accidental causation.

Key Terms
جهل (jahl): Simple ignorance: the absence of knowledge in the soul, not a positive state. [Note: In later epistemology, jahl may denote both absence of knowledge and false belief; here it is specifically absence.]
بسيط (basit): Simple; not composed. Here refers to ignorance as a simple privation. [Note: Stable in philosophical terminology.]
تكافؤ الحجج (takafu' al-hujaj): Equivalence of proofs; a situation where arguments for and against a proposition are equally strong, leading to doubt. [Note: Term used in dialectic and later by skeptics; standard in Islamic logic.]
بالذات (bi al-dhat): Essentially; what a thing does by its own nature. [Note: Standard Aristotelian distinction between essential and accidental causation.]
بالعرض (bi al-'arad): Accidentally; what occurs incidentally as a side effect. [Note: Standard term.]

Checkpoint

D) It always clarifies the truth simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Syllogisms essentially invalidate falsehood; any resulting ignorance is accidental, not caused by the syllogism itself.

Immediate Premises in Demonstration vs. Dialectic and Rhetoric
Author explains that demonstration requires immediate premises, unlike dialectical and rhetorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish demonstrative syllogisms from dialectical and rhetorical ones based on the nature of their premises.

Core Idea
Only demonstrative syllogisms need absolutely immediate premises; dialectic and rhetoric rely on accepted opinions.

This passage clarifies a key epistemological distinction in logic: what counts as a starting point for certain knowledge vs. persuasion. The author explains that the demonstrator must use premises that are self-evident or immediate within the science, while the dialectician and rhetorician can use commonly accepted or probable premises. This sets the foundation for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three syllogism types
The passage mentions demonstrative, dialectical, and rhetorical syllogisms. Notice that each type has a different kind of premise: immediate (no middle term) for demonstration, common opinion (مشهورات) for dialectic, and probable opinion (ظن) for rhetoric.
2
Understand 'immediate premises' (لا وسط لها)
A premise with no middle term means it is self-evident or taken as a first principle within that science. The demonstrator's analysis must break down to such premises, either absolutely or relative to the science.
Immediate here does not mean 'obvious' but rather 'not requiring a middle term for proof'.
3
Compare dialectical and rhetorical premises
Dialectical premises are widely accepted (e.g., 'justice is beautiful') and are treated as immediate only within the debate, not in reality. Rhetorical premises are based on probable opinion.
The example shows that even if a premise is accepted in debate, a scientist would seek a middle term to prove it.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic science
Consider a kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' The premise 'the world is temporally originated' might be immediate (through observation) or dialectically accepted. This determines whether the argument is demonstrative or dialectical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثلاثة كما مر أن هذا ثابت لذلك، وذلك لغيره؛ ولا بد من أن ينتهي إلى مباد وأصول موضوعة موجبة أو سالبة لا وسط لها على الإطلاق أو في ذلك العلم. والمبرهن مأخذ المقدمات الأولى على أن لا وسط لها بأحد هذين الوجهين وتنحل أجزاؤه إلى ما لا وسط له مطلقا؛ وإنه له القياس إما على الظن وهو الخطابي، وإما على الرأي المشهور وهو الجدلي فليس يجب أن ينتهي تحليل قياسهما إلى مقدمات غير ذات وسط في الحقيقة بل إذا انتهت إلى المشهورات أو المقبولات مثل أن العدل جميل، والظلم قبيح؛ وإنه مأخوذ في الجدل على أن لا وسط له وفي العلوم يطلب لذلك وسط. ثم هذه وما هي لها هذه الأوساط متناهية؛ وإنها إما بالذات وإما بالعرض على ما عرف.
Three as has passed that this is fixed for that, and that for other; and it must end in first principles and postulates, affirmative or negative, which have no middle term absolutely or in that science. The demonstrator takes the first premises as having no middle term in one of these two ways, and his analysis decomposes into that which has no middle term absolutely. And for him the syllogism is either based on opinion, which is rhetorical, or based on common opinion, which is dialectical. So the analysis of their syllogisms does not have to end in premises that are really immediate, but when they end in common opinions or accepted premises, such as 'justice is beautiful' and 'injustice is ugly', and it is taken in dialectic as having no middle term, while in the sciences a middle term is sought for that. Then these and what are for them these middle terms are finite; and they are either by essence or by accident, as has been known.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of demonstration and its distinction from other types of reasoning. It begins by stating that demonstration must ultimately depend on first principles that are immediate—i.e., have no middle term—either absolutely or within the particular science. The author then clarifies that the demonstrator (المبرهن) takes these first premises as immediate in one of these two senses, and the analysis of a demonstrative argument breaks down into immediate propositions. In contrast, for rhetorical and dialectical syllogisms, the premises are not required to be absolutely immediate; they may be based on common opinion (مشهورات) or accepted premises (مقبولات), which are taken as having no middle term only within the dialectical context. The example given is 'justice is beautiful' and 'injustice is ugly'—these are widely accepted but not necessarily proven. The passage then notes that the middle terms in demonstration are finite and are either essential or accidental, which sets up the subsequent discussion on predication.

Key Terms
المبرهن
لا وسط لها
القياس الجدلي
القياس الخطابي

Key Takeaway
Demonstrative syllogisms must reduce to immediate premises, whereas dialectical and rhetorical syllogisms can rely on accepted or probable opinions.

The Finitude of Terms in Demonstrative Chains
Author argues that the chain of predicates and subjects in demonstration must be finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the chain of predicates and subjects in a demonstration must be finite, and distinguish between ascending and descending limits.

Core Idea
Demonstration cannot depend on an infinite chain of middle terms — it must be finite.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument from the Islamic logical tradition: demonstration requires a finite chain of predicates and subjects. The author, likely following Aristotle's 'First Teaching', sets up three inquiries to show that infinite regress would make demonstration impossible. We will walk through each inquiry step by step, clarifying the concepts of ascending and descending from a limit, and the difference between one-sided and two-sided chains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two directions of predication chains
The author distinguishes 'ascending from a limit' (moving upwards to more general predicates) and 'descending from a limit' (moving downwards to more specific subjects). In the first inquiry, we fix a subject and ask about predicates; in the second, we fix a predicate and ask about subjects.
Think of the subject as the starting point for ascending predicates.
2
Understand the first inquiry: predicate chain from a fixed subject
The author posits a subject 'ج' (that cannot become an accidental predicate) and a series of predicates: 'هـ' predicated immediately on 'ج', 'ر' on 'هـ', etc. The question is whether this series of predicates is finite or infinite. The intended answer: it must be finite.
If infinite, there would be no first predicate to ground the demonstration.
3
Understand the second inquiry: subject chain from a fixed predicate
Similarly, from a fixed predicate that cannot have anything predicated on it by essence, we descend to more specific subjects: it predicates on 'ط', 'ط' on 'ج', 'ج' on 'ز'. The series of subjects must also be finite.
This is the mirror case of the first inquiry.
4
Grasp the third inquiry: two-sided chain between two terms
Here the author considers a proposition where 'أ' is predicated on 'ج' via a middle term 'ب'. The question is whether there can be infinite middle terms between 'أ' and 'ب' (or 'ج' and 'ب'). If so, the demonstration would depend on an infinite number of prior demonstrations, which is impossible.
This is the most important inquiry for demonstration: the chain of middle terms must be finite.
5
Recognize the exception: convertible terms
The author notes that this inquiry does not apply to terms that convert (e.g., 'man' and 'rational animal'), because such terms are mutually predicable and do not have a fixed first subject or second predicate.
Convertible terms are essentially definitions and do not generate a real predicamental chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال: محمول بالذات إذا لم يكن من شأنه أن يفارق الشيء في حكاية ما قيل في التعليم الأول من تناهي أجزاء القياسات وأوساط الموجب والسالب، قيل قد علم أن المحمولات بذاتها موجودة، والموضوعات بذاتها موجودة، فليكن موضوع بذاته «ج» وليس من شأنه أن يصير محمولا بالعرض، وليكن حمل (هـ) على (ج) أولا بلا وسط وكذلك (ر) على (ه) و (ب) على «ز»، أفترى أن هذه المحمولات من الموضوع الأول متناهية أو غير متناهية؟ ثم ليكون ليس من شأنه أن يحمل عليه شيء آخر بالذات لكنه محمول على «ط» بلا وسط، و«ط» على «ج» كذلك. وكذلك «ج» على «ز»، أفترى أن هذه الموضوعات من المحمول الأول متناهية أو غير متناهية؟ والفرق بين البحثين هو فرق ما بين الصعود من حد والهبوط من حد وليكن ( أ ) محمول على «ج» بتوسط «ب» سواء كان الألف محمول عليه، أو عليه محمول؛ و (ج) لا موضوع له، أو له موضوع فهل يمكن أن يكون بين «أ» وبين «ب» أوساط موضوعات له «أ»، أو محمولات على «ب» بلا نهاية؟ وكذلك بين «ج» و«ب». والفرق بين هذا البحث وبين الأولين بأن المحدود فيهما طرف واحد. وفي هذا طرفان وإنما يطلب هل الوسائط بينهما بغير نهاية فيكون هذا البرهان متوقف في الصحة على براهين غير متناهية؛ وليس هذا و«ج» موجب لها «أ» فقط وإن كانت مقدمة «أج» سالبة؛ والمتوسط «ب» فصارت «بج» موجبة: فهل دائما بين «أج» واسطة؟ وهذا الطلب لا يكون في الأشياء التي تستحق أن ينعكس بعضها على بعض وإن كانت من الأشياء كذلك في الحمل بالحقيقة؛ وليس فيها موضوع أول، ومحمول ثان بل كل واحد منهما يصلح أن يكون محمولا وموضوعًا أو واسطة بينهما بل يكون معا في الحالين جميعا أنه مثل يوجد لما وضع منهما
And it is said: a predicate by essence if it is not apt to separate from the thing, as was said in the First Teaching about the finiteness of the parts of syllogisms and the middle terms of the affirmative and negative. It was said: it is known that predicates by essence exist, and subjects by essence exist. So let there be a subject by essence 'J', and it is not apt to become a predicate accidentally, and let the predicate (H) be predicated on (J) first without a middle term, and likewise (R) on (H), and (B) on 'Z'. Do you think that these predicates from the first subject are finite or infinite? Then let there be that which is not apt to have anything else predicated on it by essence, but it is predicated on 'T' without a middle term, and 'T' on 'J' likewise, and likewise 'J' on 'Z'. Do you think that these subjects from the first predicate are finite or infinite? The difference between the two inquiries is the difference between ascending from a limit and descending from a limit. And let (A) be predicated on 'J' by means of 'B', whether (A) is predicated on it or it is predicated on it; and 'J' has no subject or has a subject. Is it possible that between (A) and (B) there are middle terms that are subjects for A or predicates on B without end? And likewise between 'J' and 'B'. The difference between this inquiry and the first two is that in them the limit is one side, while in this it is two sides. For it is asked whether the middle terms between them are infinite, so that this demonstration would depend for its validity on infinite demonstrations; and not this and 'J' is affirmative for it 'A' only even if the premise 'AJ' is negative; and the middle 'B' so 'BJ' becomes affirmative. Is there always a middle term between 'AJ'? And this inquiry does not occur in things that deserve to convert upon each other, even if they are from things that are so in real predication; and there is no first subject and second predicate, but each of them is suitable to be predicate and subject or a middle term between them, but they are together in both states, like what exists for what is posited from them.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage presents a sophisticated logical argument about the finitude of terms in a demonstrative chain. It begins by recalling a principle from Aristotle's First Teaching (likely the Prior Analytics) that the parts of syllogisms and their middle terms are finite. The author then sets up a thought experiment: suppose we have a subject by essence 'ج' that cannot become an accidental predicate. Let 'هـ' be predicated on 'ج' immediately (without a middle term), 'ر' on 'هـ', and 'ب' on 'ز'. The question is whether the series of predicates from the first subject is finite or infinite. Similarly, consider a term that cannot have anything else predicated on it by essence but is itself predicated on 'ط' immediately, and 'ط' on 'ج', and 'ج' on 'ز' — is the series of subjects from the first predicate finite or infinite? The author distinguishes two directions: ascending from a limit and descending from a limit. Then a third inquiry is considered: let 'أ' be predicated on 'ج' via middle term 'ب'. If 'ج' has no subject or has a subject, is it possible that between 'أ' and 'ب' there are infinite intermediate terms? The difference from the first two inquiries is that here we have two limits (the two terms of the proposition) and we ask whether the middle terms between them are infinite. If they are, then the demonstration would depend on an infinite number of demonstrations, which is impossible. The passage also notes that this inquiry does not apply to terms that can convert (i.e., interchangeable subjects and predicates like 'man' and 'rational animal'), because in such cases there is no fixed first subject and second predicate. The overall argument is a classic one for the finitude of the predicamental chain in demonstration.

Key Terms
التعليم الأول (al-ta'lim al-awwal): The First Teaching, referring to Aristotle's Prior Analytics or Organon. [Note: In the Islamic tradition, it often refers specifically to the study of syllogism.]
موضوع بذاته (mawdu' bi dhatihi): A subject that exists by itself, i.e., a substance, and is not an accident.
صعود من حد (su'ud min hadd): Ascending from a limit — moving from a more specific subject to more general predicates in the chain.
هبوط من حد (hubut min hadd): Descending from a limit — moving from a more general predicate to more specific subjects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Are the terms convertible?
C) Are the terms essential?
D) Are the terms immediate?

Key Takeaway
A valid demonstration requires that the chain of predicates and middle terms be finite, because infinite regress would make demonstration impossible.
